TO HAVE AMERICAN LAWS SO EXTENDED AS

Embassador From the Court of King Victor Emmanuel De
clares a Cash Indemnity ;Would Not Be Considered
Adequate Restitution for Recent Outrage in Erwin, Miss.
WASHINGTON. Dec. 25.â Tho
Italian Government has been
renewing its efforts of late
â¢with the authorities in Wash
ington to have our Federal
statutes so extended as to protect for
eigners in cases arising within the Juris
diction of States, thus giving a Federal

directed against Italians, such as have
occurred in Louisiana, Colorado and Mls
fissippl. It is the exchange of views re
cently which brought on the discussion

Treaty Proves One-Sided.
"Under the treaty which exists between
Italy and the United States the citizens
of each country are guaranteed the full
est protection while residing in the other
country. In the case of Americans re
tidlng in Italy they have exactly the same
rights, thÂ© same protection, the same re
course to the courts that fin Italian sub
ject enjoys. The only exception is in con-
Eectilon with political rights. In the case
of Italians residing in the United States
the treaty assures them the same rights
and protection given to American citi
zens. But. unfortunately, this expression
of thÂ© treaty, so far as it relates to Ital
ians in this country. Is somewhat theo
retical, for under the American system.,
which limits the Federal authority over
States, the central government is not
able to give effect to the guarantee laid
down by the treaty. We fully recognise
the difficulties of this situation, which
been as apparent to the statesmen
this country as they have to those of
Italy. "We have hoped this would bring
about the enactment of a law which
would permit the Federal Government to
carry out the guarantees of the treaties.
This would be a genuine remedy for af
fairs similar to those which hayÂ© oc
curred in Louisiana and Mississippi, and
would prevent the periodical agitation

VENEZUELA
INCURS IRE
OF FRANCE
Castro Involves the Re
public in a Fresh
Complication-
Seizes Property Leased to a
Subject of the Paris Gov
ernment.
Prompt Notice Is Served That a Strict
Accounting Will Be De
manded Because of
the Outrage.
Special cable to The Call and New York Her
ald. Copyright. J901. by the Herald Pub-
1 'shine company.
FORT-OF-SFAIN, Dec. 25.â Another
complication has arisen between France
and Venezuela owing to President Cas
; tro's proclivity In getting into hot water
whenever an occasion is offered. With a
revolution on his hands, with Germany
preparing to enforce her claims by a na
val demonstration in "Venezuelan waters
and with Colombia anxious for his over
throw, Castro has added another to the
grievances of France against the Vene
zuelan Government, because French citi
zens' rights have been violated.
This new grievance arises from the
seizure of the properties of Senor Manuel
H. Matos, leader of the new Venezuelan
revolution and a man of wealth. Senor
Matos leased a part of his properties to
M. Secrestat of Bordeaux for two years,
under the Venezuelan Civil Code, which
permits such a transaction to be effected
without â  the necessity, of .- registering the
documents.".- By the seizure, of the leased
properties President; Gaitro- has- deprived
M. Secrestat of their revenue and use. , rx>
France ' Takes Prompt Action.
The French Government has notified
President Castro of the Infringement of
M. Secrestat' s rights and that It intends
to protect them to the fullest extent by
demanding damages and interest If the
revenues are not Immediately paid over
to M. Secrestat. France has made a com
plete investigation and has learned that
the demand, even under Venezuelan law,
is well founded.
Venezuela has been on bad terms with
France since the rupture of diplomatic
relations in 1S95, because the South Amer
ican republic would not pay French claims
growing out of revolutions.
Germany intends to act immediately
against President Castro's Government
for the latter's failure to pay interest on
the debt owing German bankers during
the last three years, and also Its failure
to settle other claims of subjects of the
German Emperor. The German claims
arising out of the late revolution amount
to about 2,000,000 bolivars, and It is said
that $2,000,000 is due for coupons not paid
on the German loan.
The naval demonstration, it is said,
would have taken place by this time had
it not been that the German cruiser VI
neta needed repairs and had to go to the
United States for an overhauling. It is
believed that within a short time Presi
dent Castro will find a German squadron
making an effective demonstration In
Venezuelan waters and that a Venezuelan
port has been seized.
Germany's Naval Demonstration.
WASHINGTON, Dec. 25.â United States
and German warships are converging on
Venezuela. Three of the German vessels
are at St. Thomas and a fourth is at
Port-of-Spaln, Trinidad. They are ex
pected to shortly assemble and, In squad
ron formation, move on and occupy a
Venezuelan port.
In order that the United States' inter
ests, may be adequately protected, the
United States has gathered in the Carib
bean Sea a force more formidable even
than that which destroyed Spanish naval
power in the Western Atlantic. The bat
tleships Kearsarge, Alabama and Massa
chusetts are at Havana and will sail to
morrow to continue their cruise, which
will carry them very near to the Vene
zuelan coast. The battleship Illinois, now
at Algiers, La., will join them within a
few weeks. The battleship Indiana will
remain at Curacao. 170 miles from La
Guayra, and If the revolution in Vene
zuela menaces United States interests she
will hurry, to Venezuelan waters; if not,
she will join the squadron.
Besides the battleships, the United
States flag files from the masts of two
gunboats and three training ships now in
the Caribbean Sea. They will be rein
forced by seven more training ships be- j
fore the winter is at an end. The Gulf i
of Parla, a magnificent sheet of water
which washes the shore of Venezuela,
will be the destination of most of these
ships.
The authorities have not- lost sight of
the advantage of having warships in
close proximity to Venezuela. Complica
tions dangerous' to the United States may
follow a German demonstration in Vene
zuelan waters, and the administration de
sires to be prepared to meet them.
The Navy; Department received a tele
gram to-day announcing that the train
ing-ship Prairie would leave Tompklns
ville at daylight for Hampton Roads,
whence she will proceed direct to Port
of-Spaln, or Trinidad Island, just off the
north coast of Venezuela,
FIGHT STARTS IN DAVE TARPEY'S SALOON,
BUT SOON BECOMES GENERAL FOR SEVERAL
HUNDRED PROTECTORS OF THE NATION
William Ross Is Stabbed in
Back but Not Seriously
Hurt.
NOT much of peace on earth nor
.good will toward men was dis
played yesterday afternoon by
more than 1000 howling soldiers In
front of Dave Tarpey's saloon, on
Baker street, near Greenwich, just
outside the Presidio grounds. They
were men from several regiments,
cursing, yelling, demanding blood
and mixing in a general free-for-all
SCENES IN THE NEIGHBORHOOD OF DAVE TARPEY'S SALOON ON BAKER STREET. WHERE A RIOT
STARTED YESTERDAY AFTERNOON, IN WHICH 1000 SOLDIERS PARTICIPATED AND WHICH WAS
FINALLY QUELLED BY THE \ POLICE AND ARMED GUARDS.
i . â¢â  â  c â  . â  â  â  â  â  â 
fight. The riot was finally quelled by
the police and several detachments of
troops from the Presidio. Revolvers,
clubs, stones and mud were brought Into
play during the riot, and for their partici
pation fifty-nine of Uncle Sam's warriors
are under arrest in the reservation and
fifteen of them are lodged In the North
End police station. '
The wonder of the riot Is _ that no one
was seriously injured. Dave Tarpey's sa
loon received a shake-up that brought
back recollections of the old Tennessee
days. Not satisfied, with .tearing 'down
bottles, demolishing furniture and trying
to make debris out of Tarpey's place, the
infuriated . mob of soldiers moved down
Bakervetreet to Greenwich and up Green
wich to Lyon, where P. W. Anderson has
a saloon. Windows were smashed here
and a few shots were fired into the place
Just by way of variety. At this juncture
the police arrived and under Sergeant El
lis did effective work In dispersing the
rioters.
Fight Centers Around Ross.
On one of the cots in the post ; hospital
at the Presidio lies Private William Rossi
suffering from a cut In the back.' Around
Ross the disturbance of the day centers.
About' 3 : o'clock, in the afternoon he was
â¢walking up Baker street with a pail on
his arm when he noticed two soldiers
fighting on the sidewalk. Ross, who is a
, private In the Seventieth Coast Artillery,
â¢;...*â¢;â :
interfered. The street brawlers belonged
to the Eleventh Cavalry and one of them
vowed vengeance on Ross for taking a
hand. Ross helps to serve drinks in Tar
pey's saloon whenever he can get away
from post duty. Early In the morning he
ejected two bellicose corporals of the
Eleventh Cavalry for rowdyism and
threats of destruction.
Liquor flowed freely just outside the
precincts of. the Presidio all day long and
when the early hours of the evening
were reached there was fire and riot in
the air. The corporal of the Eleventh
that vowed vengeance on Ross collected
a few of his comrades together and then
they went looking for. their enemy. Ross
was in Tarpey's saloon waiting on custom
ers" at the time. Dave Tarpey himself
was sitting behind the counter, reading a
paper when the soldiers entered his place.
Ross moved out in front of the bar to
draw a glass of beer when a corporal of
the Eleventh Cavalry rushed toward him,
caught him. about the neck and stabbed
him In the back. Ross fought until he
could fight no longer. Chairs were bran
dished, bottles thrown about and clubs
used with reckless disregard for heads or
furniture. Tarpey saved Ross from fur
ther injur'y. by dragging him out through
the . rear door of the saloon and having
him carried over to the post hospital.
Riot Quickly Follows.
â¢ The soldiers meanwhile . had rushed out

Policeman George Brown Is
Severely Cut Over the
Eye.
of Tarpey'a, but they soon returned
threatening vengeance and punishment
on the head of the proprietor If he
brought charges against them or divulged
the names of any who took part in the
fracas. News of the row traveled fast
and in a very short time choice pugilistic
representatives from various regiments
were on hand. ready for further violence.
Ross was forgottten in the frenzy and
the soldier mob was ready for anything.
One man had a rope in his hand, another
was on the lookout for kerosene. Stone3
were thrown at windows and at men.
The "casuals," recruits who will soon be
on their way to Manila, came down from
the old site of the Tennessee regiment
and Joined in the melee.
Police Officers Hostetter. Rice and Her
ring, whose details He immediately out
side the Presidio grounds, rang for help
and Sergeant Tom Ellis of the North End
station I responded with the wagon and
Officers Frary, Brown, Riley, Alvares,
Rooker, Meagher and Tlerney. The fight
between the police and the soldiers was
a hot one. Stones wrought the greatest
damage and every officer engaged has a
mark or two to show for the .conflict.
George O. Brown came out of the strug
gle with a deep gash over his eye and
was taken tq_ the hospital. Sergeant El
lis with his men managed to gather in
fifteen of the soldiers and they were hur
ried up toward the North End station.
They are by name: James Doyle, John
Miller. J. F. Kinman. William Edward?.
M. E. Sullivan. Ernest v Rodgers. Roaco
Lounsbergr, John J. Jones. Vames Taylor,
E. H. Christopher. Walter* Stevrn. Sam
Hueston. Frank L. Morris. Dave Stan
ford and John Stepord. HPtf

driven by the police, clubs in hand. OpÂ«

RAILROAD TRAIN RUNS
INTO A BURNING BRIDGE
Five of the Passengers Are Badly In
jured and Only One of the
Cars Is Jsaved.
DALLAS, Tex., Dec. 23. â TSie north
bound Texas, Kansas and Pacific "flyer,"
oue at 11 p. m., ran into a burning bridge
five miles south of this city just before
midnight last night. There was no I0S3
of life, but five persons were injured and
four cars were destroyed, only the Pull
man beinjf saved.
The injured: J. S. Clark, engineer, Den
nlson, shoulder and ankle broken; Fire
man William Kearney, Dennison, cut on
lit-ad and severely bruised; Joel Henry,
colored, internally; Mrs. Rose Lull, col
ored, Waco, slignily; unknown colored
woman, -slightly.
On rounding a curve at Seven-mile
Creek Engineer Clark saw a burning
bridge ahead. He reversed iiis engine,
applied the brakes and jumped, as also
dm the fireman. The engine crossed the
bridge, but the baggage car went down.
Tlais derailed the engine, which, after
tearing up the track for some distance,
turned over into a ditch. The train be
hind the baggage car was left standing
on the track and caught lire from the
burning bridge. The passengers were
able to escape from the train by jumping
down a twelve-foot embankment. - The
contents cf the mail, express and bag
gage cars were saved. The Pullman was
rolled out of danger by the crew and-pas
sengers, but the remainder of the train,
including a chair car. two coaches and a
combination mail and baggage car, was
consumed.

PROPOSE TO STRIKE OUT
THE MINIMUM GRAINS DUTY
Germany Is Yielding to Russia's Un
compromising 1 Attitude Regard
ing Tariff Bill.
BERLIN, Dec. 25.â The Government,
yielding to Russia's uncompromising atti
tude regarding the tariff bill, will propose,
when the committee having the matter In
charge meets, that the minimum grains
duty be stricken out. These minimum du
ties were put in by the agrarians,
who desired to fix a limit under which
ccmcessions could not be made- in the

The report stated that six designs had
been submitted and Schmid's model was
considered the most appropriate. Other
competitors were Robert I. Aitken t>f San
Francisco, F. D. Jackson of San Leandro,
Schwind E. Schmitt of this city, Jean
La Veau of San Jose and Miss Sibyl Eas

decided to take the Schmid model.
The figure shows McKinley with an
overcoat as part of his attire, his hat in
his left hand and his right hand extended
in the form of welcome. ' There is much
minor elaboration which adds to the at
tractiveness of the statue.
The monument will be erected In St.
James Park, at the spot where McKinley
addressed the people of the city last May.
It will cost $13,500. All artists who sub
mitted models were allowed $25 eacb for
expenses.
MEN OF SCIENCE
EARN REWARDS
Win Fortunes Through
Discoveries Benefit
ing Mankind.
WASHINGTON, Dec. 25.-United States
Minister Thomas has reported to the State
Department that events of unusual Im
portance and interest took place on De
cember 10 in Stockholm and Christiania
in connection with the award of the No
bel prizes. Alfred Nobel, the discoverer
of dynamite, who died five years ago, lett
th'e whole of his vast fortune for the ben
efit of mankind, and in. his will directed
that it be divided into prizes, without re
gard to nationality, the worthiest to be
awarded the prize, whether he be Scandi
navian or not. The prizes consisted of
five allotments, each of more than $40,000.
The awards were as follows:
In physics, Wilhelm Conrad Roentgen
professor at the University of Munich,
the discoverer of the Roentgen rays: in
chemistry,. to. Jacobus Heinricus Vanthoff,
professor at the University of Berlin; in
medicine, to Emil von Behring, professor
at Halle, the discoverer of diphtheria se
rum; in literature, to Sully-Prudhomme,
member of the French Academy. The
works of peace prizes were divided be
tween Frederick Passy, national econo
mist, of France, and Henri Dunant of
Switzerland, the leading spirit in bringing
about the Geneva convention and insti-Â»
tutlng the societies of the^ed Cross.
The prize diplomas were awarded by the
Crown Prince in Stockholm in the pres
ence of. a great .gathering of distinguished
persons, and in Christiania the award
was made by the Norwegian Storthing,
convened in session. Sully r Prud
homme was unable to attend because of
sickness.

Nurso in a Now York Hospital BÂ«

NEW YORK. Dec. 25.â Miss Madeline
Bammons, a nurse in the Laura Franklin
Hospital for Crippled Children, was so
badly burned while playing Santa Claus
on Christmas eve at the hospital that sho
died to-day.
Miss Sammons dres3ed as Santa Claus
In a costume of light material. After all
the children had been seated and were
ready to receive presents Miss Sammons,
looking like a real Santa Claus, entered
the room. She had given presents to two
or three children, when as .quick as a
flash her costume was ablaze. The chil
dren were horror stricken. The nurses
did as much as they could to smother tho
flames, but the material was so light It
burned like tinder.

PASS CHRISTMAS
IN QUIET WAY
President and Family the
Guests of Commander
Cowles.
Public and Private Charities
Care for the Poor of
Several Citiea
WASHINGTON, Dec. 25.-The President,
Mrs. Roosevelt and the children, Robert
Ferguson and another friend took Christ
mas luncheon with Commander and Mrs.
Cowles at the latter's residence on N
street.
The party remained at the house sev
eral hours and then the male members,
including two of the Roosevelt boys, and
joined ' by several other friends, among
whom was Secretary Root, went for a
horseback ride in the outskirts of the
city.
To-night therq was quite a gathering at
the family dinner, which included the
Presidential household. Commander and
Mrs. Cowles arid other guests. After
dinner the entire party adjourned to the
east room, where the evening was spent,
the guests being entertained with a med
ley of popular airs. â 
The dinner was followed by a musical
promenade and afterward, in honor of
Theodore Rooseveit Jr., the evening was
closed with a dance, in which about
twenty couples Unished Christmas night.
NEW, YOriK, L>ec. 25.â Public and pri
vate charity gave happiness to thousands
of persons in this city to-day, while in
the outlying districts, where tne wealthy
have their homes, the poor were better
cared for and made happier than ever
before.
The feature oit the day -was the or
ganized work of the Salvation Army. By
means of a fund raised by that organiza
tion 3000 persons sat down this evening to
a substantial dinner in Madison-scjuare
Garden, while 'earlier in the day 3400 bas- ;
kets, each containing a complete dinner, |
were distributed at the garden. These j
baskets are estimated to have contained
food for 20,000 persons.
At the Bowery Mission nearly 10,000 men
and boys were fed and entertained. Vari
ous other organizations supplied all the
applicants with clothing and toys for the
children.
"All the churches held special services,
with elaborate musical programmes, and
despite a chilly, drizzling rain the day is
believed to have been a record breaker in
the way of charitable work.
CLEVELAND, Dec. 25.â The Salvation
Army gave a Christmas dinner to 5000 peo
ple in this cit>vto-day. Two thousand
were served in. Central Armory and bas
kets of provisions were sent to the re
maining 3000. At the armory Colonel Sly
ron T. Herrick made a brief address on
the work of the#Saivation Army.
CANTON, Ohio, Dec. 25.â Mrs. McKin
ley spent Christmas day alone at her
home. She was urged to join the Barber
family at their Christmas dinner in the
old Saxton home, but declined to do so.
She did not wish to be so forcibly re
minded of the Christmas days of the past
as she would be byÂ»such a dinner. â¢
Mrs. McKinley's condition is as good as
or better than it has been since the trag
edy. Relatives who see her most often
say that she shows gradual improvement.
She remembered many of her friends with
little Christmas presents to-day, and she
received flowers and presents from all
over the country.

given a silver quarter as a present.

don was fairly fine. The King, with

King Edward sent contributions to the
dinners of all of the household troops.

charitable funds.

WALTER MOROSCO DIES SUDDENLY
AT HIS UPPER FRUITVALE HOME
Former 'San Francisco Theatrical - Manager
Passes Away, Supposedly From Heart
Diseaseâ- Absent Sons Hastily Summoned
DEATH came suddenly last night
to "Walter Morosco, the . noted
theatrical man. while â¢ he was
sleeping at his residence, Ger
anium 'place, in Upper Fruitvale.
Mr. Morosco had gone to his room to
rest after enjoying the holiday with his
brothers, Edwin A. Bishop and Henry
Bishop of Chicago, and his nephews, Ed
win J. Bishop and Willard Parry.
Mr. Parry was sitting in an adjoining
room and heard his uncle breathing heav
ily. The young, man went to the bedroom
at S o'clock and noticed that Mr. Morosco
was apparently not breathing. Closer
examination revealed the fact that death
had made its claim. Dr. E. S. Depuy,
who had been attending Mr. Morosco for
a recent slight illness, was called. The
doctor could only say that death had pre
ceded him. ",.
Mr Morosco had been feeling indis
posed for several days, but he was not
so seribuslv ill as to occasion any. alarm.
His sudden death was a severe shock to
those that had been enjoying his hospi
tality. Plans had been made for a gala
time at his home during the Christmas
holidays. .
So far as is now known death came
from heart disease.

time of his death, Harry Morosco was
FAMILY CAUGHT
IN BURNING HOME
V
Husband and Wife Jump
From Window to Save
Their Lives. , .
PHILADELPHIA; Dec. 25.â One child
was burned â¢ to death, its parents were
seriously injured and two other young
children were slightly hurt as a result
of a fire at the home of James F. Con
nolly, 3976 Wyalusing avenue, at midnight
to-night. Shortly after the family had re
tired lire was discovered on the first floor
of the house. The flames had gained con
siderable headway and escape by ! the
stairs was cut off. Connolly threw his
two-year-old son and two-months-old
baby out of a second-story window into
the arms of neighbors and the little ones
escaped with slight injuries.
The parents then jumped and were both
badly hurt by striking on their heads on
the stone sidewalk. In the excitement the
four-year-old daughter, Mary, was for
gotten. When the parents realized that
she had not escaped a frantic effort was
made by the firemen to save her. The
house by this time was badly damaged
and after the flames had* been extin
guished the little charred body was found
in a second-story rear room.* It is not
yet known whether the parents will re
cover from their injuries.
Trouble Over Mining' Claims.
' GUTHRIE, O. T., Dec 25.â United
States Indian Agent Randlett of the
Kiowa and Comanche tribes is sending
out notice to all persons who have staked
mining claims in Indian allotments that
they must vacate at once or they will be
ejected by Fort Sill troops. The action is
based upon the opinion of the Attorney
General, approved by Secretary Hitch
cock and sent to the agent under recent
date, prohibiting miners from filing on
Indian allotments.

WELL KNOWN -THEATRICAL MAN
â¢ WHO DIED SUDDENLY YESTER
DAY IN HIS FRUITVALE HOME.
summoned quickly from San Francisco.
Dispatches calling Leslie -Morosco home
from Seattle and Oliver Morosco from Los
Angeles were sent at once.
Mr. Morosco's long and profitable con
nection with San Francisco theatricals
gave him prominence throughout the
United States. He was born about fifty
five years ago at Gullford, Connecticut,
his family name being Bishop. As a young
man he joined a troupe of acrobats, styled
/'the Morosco brothers," and for .years the
circuses of the country had the team on
their bills. Mr. Morosco abandoned the ,
circus and embarked in the theatrical
business. His first move was a remarka
ble one in the history of San Francisco
theatricals. He opened a playhouse in old
Union Hall, then on, Ho ward ;, street,
tween Third and Fourth. It was south of
Market street and t*Â»e old-time". theater,
owners looked with amazement upon the
move. But it was af big success, and led
to the subsequent leasing by Mr. Moros
co of the Grand Opera-house, where he
continued his successes along popular
price lines. For many years Mr. Moros
co, with his son Harry, was at the head
of the Grand Opera-house.
His Benefactions Many.
Several months ago, desiring to retire
from active service, Mr. Morosco disposed
of his theatrical holdings to- a corporation
with which his son Harry is connected.
Since his retirement Mr. Morosco had
been residing at his country home, which
is one of the beautiful places of Oakland
and its suburbs. There the retired man
ager e-njoyed comfort and rest. Among
his pleasures was driving. He was pas
sionately fond of horses, and drove often
a fine team of eight black horses to a
tally-ho. His famous collection of Shet
land ponies was Â»a great attraction. Of
large liberality',. sMr. Morosco's benefac
tions were' many: He took particular de
light in assisting "the work of the asso
ciated kindergartens of Oakland.
There will be nothing done about funeral
arrangements until the absent sons have
arrived...
The relatives are prostrated over the
sudden shock, for the death was most un
expected. Mr. Parry said last night:
"We are simply overwhelmed, for noth
ing was further from our thoughts in this
holiday time than the possibility of the
sudden taking off of my uncle."

COURT AWARDS
HAWAII LAND
Property in the Pearl
Harbor Locality Con- .
demned. â 
United States Is Successful
in First of the Law
suits.
1 HONOLULU, Dec. 18 (via" Victoria, B.
C, Dec. 25.)â The first of the suits brought
by the United States to condemn lands
needed for the naval . station at Pearl
Harbor was decided last week by a jury
in the United States District Court,
which awarded the land to the Govern
ment at a price far below the demands of
the owners..
The case was that of the United States
against the estate of Bernice Pauahi
Bishop, Involving over 600 acres of land
at* the site of the proposed station. The
Bishop estate, in its answer to the suit of
condemnation,' demanded about $600 per
acre for the land, while the Government's
price, was about ?30. The jury brought In
a verdict awarding the. defendants about
$75 per acre. The verdict is taken to indi
cate that tne Government will get land
wanted for a naval station for a small
sum.:' .
A special election held In the Hllo dis
trict last Week to choose a successor to
the late J. E. Waliko, member of. the
House- of Representatives, resulted in the
,. (-lection of James Lewis, who was the de
feated Republican candidate at the for
mer election. The campaign -was a quiet
one. " â¢ :
President Roosevelt's message is com
mented here as averse to tne sugar in
terests of Hawaii. The declaration
against allowing more Chinese to come
here was a.' disappointment to the plan
ters.*: The prospect of reciprocity with
Cuba and the possibility of tree admission
of sugar from that island and the Philip
pines, as well as Porto Rico, are also dis
appointing to Hawaii. In spite of these
inferences, however, the sugar stocks
remain firm.
Attorney General Dole will leave for
Washington in the near future to present
to the. United States Supreme Court the
appeal of the Territory in a case involv
ing'the question of the legality of trials
without Grand Jury indictments under
the Hawaiian law allowing jury verdicts
by nine jurors after an examination.
Two other suits brought by the Gov
ernment have been compromised, the
United States getting the lands without
cost. ' The John II estate agreed to and
has deeded to the Government a strip of
land amounting to twenty-three acres on
Ford- Island, and the suit against the 1 li
estate for the whole of the island has
been withdrawn, as it is held to be un
necessary to take It all. The Oahu Sugar
Company also, surrendered its leasehold
interest in the same land. The suit
against the Oahu Railway . and Land
Company for the lands covered by its
right of way has been settled. The com
pany deeds the land to the Government
with the understanding that its tracks
shall not be disturbed until a new right
of way is provided.
The most important condemnation suit
now pending is against the Honolulu
Plantation Company to secure the com
pany's leasehold interest in the lands of
the Bishop estate already condemned. A
suit against the Dowsett estate for 4SS
acres of this same land is also pending.
GENERAL GOMEZ FARES
POORLY IN POLITICS
He Is Met With Charges of Treachery
and Stones Are Thrown
at Him.
HAVANA. Dec. 25.â According to tele
grams printed in Senor Estrada Palma's
organs. General Gomez is faring badly in
his-', political matters. In' the
: Principe province his meetings have been
â¢ broken up and he has been greeted with
shouts of "Traitor" and stones have been
thrown at him. General Gomez is mount
ed, on the same horse he used during the
war and is accompanied by a band and a
number of veterans. The Maso' feeling is
running strong in Puerto Principe.
The Nacion says Gomez Is paid by the
Government to stump the country. He is
met with that accusation on every hand.
Maso's followers have withdrawn every
one of their candidates and the feeling
at some points is bitter.

STEAMER BURNS
TO WATER'S EDGE
Four Passengers. Perish
in Staterooms Before
Aid Arrives.
MEMPHIS. Tenn., Dec. 25.â The steam
er Sun, employed in the Memphis and Ful
ton trade, burned to the water's edge at
an early hour this morning and four lives
were lost. The" dead: . .f.-
D. N. RA1NOR, Old River, Arje. _
MRS. D. N. RAINOR, Old River, Ark.
MRS. G. M. TIMBS, Richardson Land
ing, Tenn. â¢ â¢ - .
Child of Mrs. G. M. Timbs. . â¢
The fire was discovered at 4:10 a. m. as
the steamer lay at her wharf here, and
spread with such rapidity that the craft
was a mass of flames when the depart
ment responded to the alarm. 'When the
Sun arrived in port last night from Ful
ton most of the passengers went ashore,
but ten or' a dozen of,. them decided to
spend the night on the boat. Among these
were Mr. ana Mrs. Timbs and baby, Mrs.
Timbs* sister, Miss Josie Hammers, and
Mr. and Mrs. D. N. Rainor. . Miss Ham
mers was sleeping with her sister and the
baby in a stateroom, and when she was
awakened by the smoke she aroused her
sister and started toward an exit. Mrs.
Timbs, however, went back for the baby,
and before she could return the hurricane
deck gave way and escape was cut off.
Miss Hammers got out without injury.
Timbs was in another stateroom, and un
til the collapse of the roof came tried by
every means to reach his wife. Mr. and
Mrs. Rainor had a stateroom, and al
though it was thought that all the pas
sengers on the boat had been saved noth
ing was heard from them until their
charred remains were discovered. "When
! the first body wasftaken out Timbs iden
tified it as his wifcjby a ring worn on her
finger. The body- was horribly burned.
The body of the baby has not been found.
The steamer Sun was the property of
George W. Phillips, who was her captain,
and John- Breiner, who acted as clerk.
The loss to steamer and cargo probably
will reach $20,000. partially Insured.

RIOTERS KEEP
OFFICERS BUSY
Serious Clash Bet^ween
Negroes and Whites
of Harlem.
'NEW YORK, Dep. 25.â A race skirmish
between whites and blacks In Harlem to
day . developed serious proportions and
kept the police busy for a time. The ne
groes, 100 strong, were armed with revolv
ers and clubs, while equally as many
whites used stones and other weapons.
The negroes had the advantage, but were
driven back into their tenements by the
police after they had charged on the
whites with pistols and razors. Fifteen
negroes were arrested. Four whites, in
cluding a policeman, were badly shot or
cut. . .
The rioting began when some white
boys stoned two colored girls, whereupon
a dozen negroes, seized the white boys and
beat them. The boys, when released, told
their story to white men in the vicinity
and an organized attack was made on the
negroes, who were driven back to their
tenements under a shower of stones. The
confusion increased when the white men
began to bombard the tenements with
stones and other missiles and the negroes
fired from their windows into the crowd.
The whites, surprised by the shooting and
seeing men fall wounded, turned and ran.
followed by the negroes, who used knives
and clubs freely.
At this point a squad of policemen ar
rived on the scene and with drawn clubs
stopped the rioting, chasing the negroes
into their houses.

The end and aim of the Incarnation anfl of the
birth of the Son of God was our salvation,

which may be said to have its beginning at
Bethlehem.

TELLS OF THE NATIVITY.
Rev. Dr. Clampett Preaches a Force
. ful Christmas Discourse.
The Christmas music at Trinity Church
was of an unusually impressive character
Rev. Frederick "W". Clampett. rector of
the church, preached from St. John, i:14:
'The Word was- made- fiosh and dwelt
among- us.' He referred to the increas
ing and widening interest of the churches
of the land in the commemoration of the
nativity of the blessed Lord. In part he
said: â  " â¢
Nothing Impresses Itself upon the mind more
than the simplicity of the story of Bethlehem.
It has been told In language of transparent
. beauty. It is an event so striking as to im
press itself upon an entire civilization. And
yet our time is reckoned upon the event, the
eyes of millions are turned in holy love upon
the Infant form ot Bethlehem, and kings and
queens bow, as the wise men of old, before
tho "Word made flesh."
He was likewise sublime.' Truly Â»he
heavens were telling the glory of God. when
the Star guided the first worshiper to Bethie
hem and the angel '.-hnrus Riled the air.
Time has demonstrated Its permanency. The
world was never more prepared to receive and
reverence the message.
Tills message of simplicity, sublimity and
permanency la valued because the child JesuÂ«
was the revelation of the Father. Nothing
short of the divine Incarnation will satisfy tlie
longing soul. Nothing short of the deity of
the Son of God. will harmonize the truths 'of
revelation. Meen may extol the Master as a
pure moralist and unique example of-self-sac
rifice, but in the supreme thought of his ab
solute deity â his oneness with the Father â
rests all that makes the Christian faith a
power. We behold God through the attributes
of Jesus. The majesty, the mercy, compassion,
pity and Infinite love of the Father were re
vtnled through 4he life of Jesus of Nazareth.
His incarnation, again, was the enrichment
of humanity. 1 It was "God manifest In the
flesh." With singular force this truth comes
to us in Chrlstmastlde. It adds sacrednesa
and dignity to manhood. It speaks to us in
the language of St. John: "Behold, now are
we the sons of God, and It doth not yet appear
what we shall be: but we- know that when he
shall appear. we shall be like him."
The Christian faith, at this season, speaks
with remarkable power of the Infinite, possi
bilities thus opened out by the life of the
child. Jesus. His humanity has sanctified all
humanity. To this end he came that we might
have r life abundantly. â '
These truths make possible all the joys' of
Christmas. Beneath social and home bleselncs
exchange of gifts and mutual well wishes, is
the one supreme truth of the incarnate Jesus
Christ, the Son of God. "The "Word," verily
"was made flesh and dwelt among- us." -

Mayor-Elect Eugene E. Schmitz Renders a
Violin Saloi at Offertory in St. Mary's
Church â Impressive Services Are Held
AT St. Mary's Cathedral the
music was a. feature, as it has
always been on holidays. The
choir and orchestra, under the
direction of Professor Harri
son, rendered an excellent pro
gramme.
At the 10:30 o'clock mass the
Rev. Chariest A. Ramm
preached the sermon. ; His discourse was
a beautiful one and was of Christmas. In
part he said:
God being a pure spirit the worship of him
is the worship of a pure spirit. Since, however,
â we have never had any experience of spiritual
being,: and cannot, thereto^, even imagine
what they are JJke. this worship of God â
which is our highest religious duty â Is neces
sarily extremely difficult. Worsip is a mental
and intellectual act; the will and mind must
produce it or it does not exist; but aa we can
not think. AX. all without the accompaniment of
figures or images lit the imagination tha
thought of God involved in hia worship must
necessarily be attended by some Image of him.
It. is the difficulty of forming this image which
makes our mental grasp' upon God commonly
so feeble. - You will at once realize what I
mean if you have- ever tried to cultivate de
votion to God the Father or to God the Holy
Ghost. To most people. I am sure, the object
of such devotion will seem illusive, vanishing
before them as they try to fix their mental eyea
upon it. What sort of a mental picture can we
form of thÂ« Holy Ghost?
This initial difficulty of worship springing
from the spirituality of God is increased by a
second, arising from the character of the wor
ship which he requires of us. Worship. I have
said. Is an intellectual act. . But it is more
than that. It is an act of our whole being.
The first and greatest commandment is this:
"Thou shalt love the Lord thy God with thy
whole soul, with thy whole heart and with thy
whole mind." Not alone our intellect, but
our feelings, our emotions and our affections
are to have a share in our worship. Without
these the purely mental acts which form the
basis of our love and adoration are- cold and
feeble. It is the heart which gives to worship
Intensity and color and warmth.
When our feelings run along with our will
religious acts are easy. When they are ab
sent or contrary it takes all our'resolutlon and
courage to remain faithful to our duty. One
half our being seems then to be rebelling
against the other half; and generally, in the
end, If the conflict be long, our mental powers
lose their grasp and we are swept away by
the persfstent current of our feelings.
Yet these feelings are a part of our strange
little being. Powerful aids they are for good
when they respond divinely to some noble in
spiration; flemon furies when perverted to the
cause of sin. They are God's gifts to us. and
he asks us to bring them to his service, so
that our whole being, spiritual ana physical,
may offer him one harmonious homage, i â 
But how can we bring our hearts to serve
him? Can the thought of the Invisible spirit.
God. who is everywhere and yet nowhere, warm
us with the glow of fervor? Can an imma
terial being kindle emotions within us? Can
the fingers of our affections twine around that
which we cannot even imagine? â 
The kind heart need3 the tangible, the vis
ible, the present, the concrete to move It. The
absence of these from the pure spirituality of
God Snade the worship of him in the olden time
doubly hard. It is for this reason that his
chosen people could with the utmost dlfflculty
be made to cleave to the theism which it was
their mission to preserve. They wanted to see
and feel their God, and so fell an easy prey
to the Idolatry of their heathen neighbors.
When- Moses delayed In coming; down from the
mountain the people forgot the Lord whose
prophet he was, -and cried out to Aaron:
"Arise, make us gods that may go before us."
It was but the strong cry of their sensible
nature drowning the somber' but weak voice
of reason. .
So it was throughout their history. They
were constantly falling away: and It was only
by the direct punishments that God could hold
them, even to a fitful, cold and feeble worship.
But surely a merciful Father will at length
have pityupon this weakness of human nature.
He will not always remain far aw&y from his
children. He will not always wile by fear;
he will come to them; be wilt win them to
himself, and hold them by the cords of Adam,
by the bonds of love.
Jesus lives to-day en every altar. He Is
there, the same gracious Savior who had com
passion on the multitude' in the wilderness;
who brought Mary Magdalen weeping and peni
tent to his feet;' -whose touch healed the sick
who comforted the grief-stricken widow of
Nain ; who wept at the tomb of Lazarus and
whose look of reproach brought the bitter tears
of repentance to the eyes of Peter.
The doors of his temple are always open,' in
viting us to enter and fall down before him
in silent adoration. There. In hia presence
our hearts grow warm again, our strength is
renewed and our courage revived. There our
difficulties of worship are solved and the Irre
pressible cravings of our nature to feel our
Savior near us find their answer. He has not
condemned us to wander over the face of the
earth, like Cain, with the curse of our slnÂ«
upon us. For we know where he la and we
may arise and go to him and cast our burden
upon him, as Martha's sister did when hÂ»
heart was heavy. u * r
Around the altar where Jesus dwells thpro
is life and warmth. There hia kneeling chil
dren adore him with the ever-present anÂ«>]Â«
In the great cold world outside his very nkmi
and life are. .las. fast becoming a merl rneâ¢

I Ruskin in his beautiful essay. "The JIj-3

rain." But as in nature there are c!outl3 in

What la this soul-fire? It is the desire for

Is this desire universal with men? Before

tinction. Potentially, yes; in actual exercise,
not always. There are both light and dark

you want? I should soon discover in every In
stance that there was something which they

ADVERTISEMENTS.
Bargain Babies.
If babies were for sale the most invet-
erate bargain-hunting woman in the
world would not look for a bargain baby.
She would want the best baby that could
be bought, regardless of price.
Every woman naturally craves a
healthy, handsome child, and her crav-
ing can be gratified if she will but re-
member that the child's health is her
own gift, and to give health she must

IS EVERY DAY ASSUMING GREATER IMPORTANCE
Immense Mineral Resources of -the Several
Northern Counties Are Giving Redding
Proud Place Among Cities of the State.
Smelters Belch Smoke Day and Night
THE phenomenal advance nf
Redding, the county seat of
Shasta County, is of moment
to the entire State. Last
year Shasta County produced
minerals, as officially report
ed by the State Mineralogist.
to the high aggregate of
55,574,026. The total for the
entire State of California during the same
period was $32,622,545. Roughly speaking.
Shasta, therefore, is to be credited with i
more than one-sixth of the whole value I
of all minerals which California added to '
the wealth of the world in the year 1900. ;
Gold, silver and copper, like a trinity of
genii slumbering in the hills of Shasta
answering to the Aladdin-like touch of
energy, came forthâ glittering and potent
â while the mountains quaked and rever
berated. Their allied wealth gladdened
the hearts of the sturdy miners of the
north and pointed the way to a future
that looks very bright.
Along the water courses, along the high
ways leading- from Redding to the coun
ties of Siskiyou, Trinity and Modoc, in
the bed of the Sacramento River and
other streams; in the depths of high
mountains, Enterprise groped her way.
The mines of an earlier and historic day,
in which the pioneers found treasure,
were again exploited for gold, but cop
per is the new giant of the north. Gold
is still a factor in Shasta and a large one.
The total output of the county in gold
last year was $733,467, and only seven coun
ties in California made a better gold
record, but the value of Shasta copper
was $4,166,735, which was more than the
values in gold output of any two counties
combined. Copper is king of the present
and near future.
Day and night, unceasingly, vaporous
cloujs float, banner-like, over the smelt
ers of Keswlck, in full view of Redding.
Something like a score of miles farther
removed the unceasing fires of Bully Hill
are reducing the flinty ores, copper bear
ing, of another belt or region â rich in
mineral wealth. On the line of the rail
road going to Oregon is Kennett, in which
there is great promise. tBalaklala is an
other name to conjure with in this fa
vored county of Shasta, The Mountain
Copper Company's operations at Keswick
end De La Mar's at Bully Hill have al
ready given rise to communities of some
.size. Other hamlets and towns, thriving
and vigorous, will come into existence In
the rear futureâ wherever and whenever
the magic hand of copper points the way
and marks the time on the dial. The pres
ent prospect Is that each and all of these
communities within a wide radius of
Redding will for a long term of years
enhance the prosperity and growth of
Redding, while other places are also pres
ent gainers. m
IN COMMANDING POSITION.
Sends Supplies to a Region of Great
Richness.
In a certain degree Redding occupies the
tame relative position to the surrounding
mining country that Sacramento held in
regard to the placers in early days. It is
a supply point and center of distribution.
Stage routes radiate in all directions.
Over the grades, up hill and down, over
bridges, by the rivers and through, the
mountains, the teamsters crack their
whips as their dusty horees carry in and
out machinery for the mines, boilers and
pumps, ores for the smelters and all the
miscellaneous supplies needed at the
mines. The stage routes from Redding
i ead to all points north in Shasta County,
"%o points in Modoc, Sigkiyou and Trinity
I ounties. At a general stage office the
following 1 named localities are enumerated
as being reached regularly by stage ser
vice from Redding:
Anderson. Adin, Alturas. Bear Valley, Bie
\>er. Burney Valley. Balls Ferry, Bella Vista,
Baird. Black Diamond, Bis Bend. Springs,
â¢ "enterville, Cauby, Carvllle, Coldridge, Can
von City. Copley, Copper City. Castella,. Cin
nabar. Delta, De La Mar. Deadwood, Douglas
Â«"Ity, Dedrick, French Gulch. ; Furna^eville,
Fall River Mills, Fort Uidwell, Harrison
*;ulch. Hatchet Creek, Horse Creek. Hay Fork.
Iron Mountain, Igro. Junction City, Keav.-lck.
Kennett Lake View, LUlienthal Lowden
Kanch. Mlllvllle. Montgomery Creek. Mfriere
ville, Morleys, Middle Creek, Montague, North
Fork, Ono, Oak Bottom, Pit River Bridge,
Palo Cedro. Pltville, Round Mountain, Shin
rietown. Shasta Stillwater, Sallee, Tower
House, Trinity Center, Watson Gulch, Weaver
vnie, Whiskytown.
With the commanding station of gate
way to the heart of a great mining region
large benefits have come to Redding dur
ing the last few years, and are still swell
ing her wealth. There is not room enough
in the city for the people who have gone
ihere recently to dwell. In the suburbs
people have been camping in wagons and
tents waiting for houses to be finished to
accommodate them. Parties who expect
ed to have houses have compromised on
having a few rooms. Nevertheless there
has been a. great deal of building- in progr
ess recently. The value estimated to be
'represented by buildings in progress at
one time very lately was. $250,000. At this
time two new hotels are being added. One
of these Is a brick four-story structure,
to contain 340 rooms. This is built with
money taken from the mines of- Trinity
County. The other new hotel mentioned is
of wood, three stories high, which stands
on the corner of Butte and Oregon streets.
The Shasta County Bank has a new and
costly home on Market street, between
Yuba and Placer. Office and store blocks
are in construction. Many cottages have
been built. Real estate agents are fore
seeing the coming of a greater population
and are placing large tracts of eligible
lands on the market for building sites.
City improvements will undoubtedly be
made on a large scale.
MOUNTAIN COPPER PLANT.
Millions in Bed Metal Annually Pro
duced at Keswick.
The nearest of the great copper plants
to Redding is that of the Mountain Cop
per Company at Keswlck. The force of
men employed there has ranged as high
as 1500. M. E. Dittmer says that the
Mountain Copper Company is producing
at the rate of $4,500,000 per year, and that
the Bully Hill Copper Mining and Smelt
ing Company, of which J. R. de la Mar
Is the moving spirit, has a present an
nual rate of production of $2,750,000, which
will .be doubled soon by the Increase of
nmelters. Every visitor to Redding who
has time at his disposal visits Keswlck.
Very few ever are permitted to enter the
grounds inclosing the great plant, but
that is not necessary to comprehend the
greatness of the enterprise, lor the hills
surround the smelters, and from any point
a good view is obtainable.
The mine is away up on the mountain
side. Thirteen miles of track are em
ployed by the railway trains to bring
ACTING MAYOR ROHM MAKES
FORECAST OF ADVANCEMENT
THERE is no boom in Redding. The conditions' are those of perma
nency. Redding relies upon the mines for her prosperity, at least in
a very large degree. The reliance Indicated is based on knowledge of
the proximity of great deposits of ore at Keswick, Bully Hill, Bala
klala, Kennett, Clear Creek, Harrison Gulch and other loc;ftities. Redding
has a great forwarding business, which is hers by^ right of situation as re
lated to the surrounding country. There are two large lumber districts trib
utary to Redding. One is centered at Bella Vista; the other is in the coun
try about Sisson.
There is no doubt that we have a progressive population in Redding. The
present Board of Trustees of the city was elected upon the programme of
bonding to furnish better streets and a sewer system. So rar that has not
been carried out, but there is no doubt that public sentiment favors putting
the .city in the best possible shape to attract outsiders of the desirable sort
to become permanent residents. A part of the programme Is to build a city
hall and to set out shade trees along the streets for beautification and also for
comfort of the passers.
This is still a comparatively new city, but there are evidences that citi
zens of the most progressive type have settled here, and that Redding has
the favoraoi- consideration of the keenest men in California. .It also has
the advantage of a world-wide reputation as the city nearest the great cop
per mines that have been opened up in this county in^the past few years.
These are factors that count for advancement. ..
We have a free reading room and library, good schools, good hotels, good
prospects in all directions and advantages to match, so that -we have no rea
son to doubt that we shall reap a full harvest and that all who make their
homes with us will have reason to be thankful that they came here to share
with us what we have.- The enterprising spirit of Reddirtg may be relied upon
to push-this city forward constantly. / J. T. ROHM,
President of Redding Board of Trustees.'

PROMISE BIG BETTJBNS.
Many Mining Properties Hold Out
Fine Promises.
It is manifestly impossible to describe
all the multitude of mining properties in
Shasta County. There are great hopes
of the future of Balaklala and Kennett.
The Mount Shasta Gold Mines Corpora
tion is operating the old Mount- Shasta
gold mine, which has a: record of having
sent $25,000 per month to . the Keswick
smelters. Among the prominent mining
districts is Harrison Gijlch, already well
known to fame. The great operations at
the Sweepstakes mine, in- Trinity County,
are bringing profit to Redding. One con
tract for teaming pipe in to the mines
amounted to $35,000, the contractor, being
a Trustee of Redding. Between Redding
and Keswick gold dredging operations are
under way. â¢
It is a great sight when the teams that
are to travel over the country roads with
supplies for distant mines and mining
camps and towns are loaded up in the
square facing the railroad freight sheds
on one side and' a principal business street
of Redding bn the other. One needs to
travel no farther to see how things were
done in the pioneer days, when men re
lied upon their own energies to cover the
distances to the mines. There have been
counted as many as 150 freighting teams
in the square in one afternoon. The men
are sunburned and hardy â tough, as a
knot, and at once plucky and good
humored. You will see them later in the
day, if you ride abroad, making their
noonday meal at some small town or by
the roadside. Redding appreciates to the
full the advantage of the possession of
this service of freighting teams to bring
business to it. Equally with, the miners,
the stage drivers and the teamsters are
making the city grow.
There Is a good story going the rounds
which illustrates the avidity with which
eligible mining properties in Shasta Coun
ty are now sought. According to this the
Mountain Copper Company had the Bala
klala mine under bond for $200,0C0. On
the day that the bond expired the man
ager of the Copper Mining Company tele
phoned the owners to come out and get
$15,000, the amount required to keep the
bond alive under renewal. They did not
respond. The money was not sent to
them: In ninety days the property was
sold for $450,000 to Philadelphians, a win
ning for the owners of $250,000 In three
months by waiting. â  \
During a recent suit in court in Red
ding Mr. Cohen, manager of the Bully
Hill property, made a statement under
oath that the profits of the Bully Hill,
were $100,000 per month; The price paid
for the property by the present owners \%
reported to have been $250,000. * â â¢.-â¢â 
One more statement of interest should
be made concerning the mines of Shasta
County as a whole. This ia derived from
the annual report of the State Mineralo
gist, who finds that the total output in
creased from $4,661,981 in 1899 to $5,574,026 in
1900, the difference in favor of the last
year being $912,045. The present year will
show another large, gain. No one believes
that the price of copper, which has fallen
as a result of manipulation, will be as low
for any length pf time as it has been're
cently. The multiplication of electrical
uses makes the demand for copper larger
all the time. Conditions will be more fa
vorable in the matter of prices after a
time, a consideration... which 1 vitally af
fects one of the largest copper belts on
earth, that of Shasta County.

Boardman and George C. Boardman, all
of whom are well known in San Fran
cisco. Mr. Parker says that he has looked
into the matter of making the road pay
and is satisfied, after a full Investigation,
that there is money in it. The route may
involve bridging the Sacramento River at
two places. Mr. Parker has been resid
ing some time In Redding, where he has
opened up the Boardman addition to Red
ding and has energetically "pushed the
sale of many lots, for which a good de
mand has been found. The Southern Pa
cific Company's road does not go near
the smelters at Keswick. Mr. 1 Parker says
that in that fact Is a promise of business
for an electric road. He also thinks it

EDITOR SWASEY SEES SIGNS
OF GREATNESS FOR REDDING
THE United States census of 1900 , placed . Redding's population ap
:" proximately at 3000. Since the census was, taken at least 1000 more
persons have taken up their residence here. Within the next five
j years we will have a population of 10,000. Mining is the leading: in
dustry. Our ores being base in their nature; their treatment never met with
much success until the smelter was introduced .by an English company
which purchased the celebrated Iron Mountain Mine, a vast deposit of cop
per, sold and silver. :
:' Since then the county has advanced from the . sixth to the leading or first
county in the State as a mineral producer. "We have .now two smelting
plants with a prospect of two more in .the near. future.: It has been posi
tively'ascertained that, beginning at Keswick, on the Sacramento River, we
have a continuous copper deposit in the form of a horseshoe, for fifteen
miles, reaching to the town of De La Mar.'near PifRiver, with spurs there
from. -...-..
Redding Is not only the center of this rich mineral belt, but is also the
distributing point for Trinity County, which has become famous for her
rich placer mines, surpassing any county in the State for her extensive and
rich gravel deposits. Add to this the timber resources of the ' county of
Shasta and the rapid and continuous growth of Redding Is certain. ,
The traveling public tells us that Redding is the best advertised city In
the State, and we expect to experience, growth in the year 1902 surpass
ing that of any five years. There is sure to be a, large and prosperous city
between Sacramento and the State line. 'and that Is going to be the City
of Redding. , P. M. SWASEY, Editor Redding Free Press.

The history of Redding Is typical. It was
laid out by the railroad surveyers and was
named â  after Joseph D. Redding,. \ once
prominent in the councils of the Southern
Pacific Company. The first railroad train
reached Redding bn the night of Sunday,
September 1, 1872. The railway office in

the new place was opened for business on
-he following day. A. S. Castle estabb
lished the first boarding accommodations,
in a large tent. Judge C. C. Bush began
the first substantial Improvement, erect
ing a frame store building on the corner
of California and Butte streets, the site
of the present Bush block.
Mhe inherent vitality of Redding was
demonstrated when the railroad was ex
tended to Oregon, which furnished a test
concerning Redding's ability to retain its
hold upon the forwarding trade. .Red
ding did not suffer in the least. The re
moval of the county seat to Redding
from Shasta was authorized by the coun
ty in 1SS8.
â An estimate is made .that 30,000,000
pounds â  of freight are annually un
loaded at the Redding depot.
Redding is lighted by electricity. The
business structures in the center of the
city are constructed of brick. The streets
are wide and easy of grade. . The Sacra
mento River is spanned by a free bridge,
which facilitates travel very largely.
From many points in the city Mount
Shasta looms up grandly and other
snowy mountains are also in sight. There
are many nice residences. The most
prominent building in the city Is the
County Courthouse. This structure was
erected in 188S at a cost of , $60,000. Two
handsome buildings are owned by fra
ternal organizations, the Temple Hotel
building being the property of the Ma
sonic Building Association, while the Odd
Fellows own a business block.
There are two sets of public officials in
Redding. The Board of Trustees of the
city consists of Dr. J. T. Rohm, president
of the board and acting Mayor; Carl R.
Briggs, H. Clineschmidt, Samuel Hill and

INTERESTS ARE VARIED.
Lumber and Cattle Industries Add to
. Shasta County's Welfare.
Mining 'is not the' only large Interest of
Shasta County. Fruit growing has been
successfully followed at Anderson, which
Is twelve miles south of Redding. -At
Bella Vista, twelve miles east from Red
ding, is the headquarters of the Terry
Lumber Company, which has a vast lum
ber output in sugar pine and yellow pine
principally. Burney Valley, situated
sixty-six miles northeast from Redding,
is a good agricultural country. There are
also agricultural and stock interests at
Burgess, fifty-five miles northeast from,
Reddirg. The Shasta King, Balaklala
and Stowell groups of mines are near
Copley, on the railroad, nine miles north
from Redding, 'on the Sacramento River.
Cottonwood is a good shipping point. It
is eighteen miles from Redding. The soil
la productive. At French Gulch there are
good mining properties. A daily stage
connection Is maintained with Redding.
There are excellent mining prospects at
Gas Point, Hart, Whltehouse, Igo, Knobâ
which latter is the postofflce name for
Harrison Qulchâ Middle Creek, Oak Run,
Shasta, Shlngletown, Whiskytown and
Still water.
The mineral springs and health resort3
of Shasta County are famous.
The Redding local papers give an ac
count of a railroad which is now project
ed for the purpose of connecting' Fall
River Mills with the Southern Pacific
main line. The company concerned in
the project la the Shasta Mineral Belt
Railway, of which r>. P. Doak of St.
Louis is the president. Two routes are
offeredâ one following the Pit River from
its junction with the Sacramento River,
three miles above Kennett, and the other
extending from Bella Vista, the terminus
of the Terry road. The purpose alleged
Is to push on through Modoc County
with this line.
The historic town of Shasta is gradual
ly being merged into the corporate exist
ence of Redding by the simple process of
migration. People who were formerly
dwellers in Shasta are now leading citi
zens in Redding. They and their house
hold goods have gone to Redding for fhe
greater number of advantages afforded by
the larger community. The stores of
Shasta are now nearly all closed. Dur
ing the last year the only hotel of Shasta
has succumbed. Various business places
have followed suit. The old-time stores
are represented only by unoccupied brick
buildings. The former store windows are
covered by iron blinds, or shutters, that
are never taken down now. A few of the
old residents remain. The oldest of these
in point of years of residence is probably
Mrs. C. N. Knox, who has resided in
Shasta continuously ever since 1852. Else
where the story is told erf the taking
away of the county seat to Redding.
Over the principal street of Shastaâ tha
town is mainly all upon one â¢ streetâ the
teamsters drive constantly on their way
to Whiskytown. Weaverville and other
points, where there ia a demand for sup
plies. Shasta Is the oldest town In North
ern California. Once it was the center of
great mining activity. No one can safely
say that it will not again become promi
nent. The copper discoveries that have
given Shasta County its present -leading
position among the mineral counties of
California were not known a few years
ago. Other discoveries are probable.
It was in Shasta, in 1850, that the first
public meeting was held at which the or
ganization of Shasta County was pro
posed. Then Shasta County embraced an
area 200 miles square, extending from the
Oregon State line on the north to Red
Bluff on the south and from the summit
of the Coast Range Mountains on the
west to the Sierra Nevada Mountains on
the east. County officers were elected for
Shasta County on October 7, 1850, which
was about one month after the bill was
passed by the National. Congress admit
ting California as a State of the Union.
Shasta was chosen as the county seat oO
February 15. 1831.
Heavy Frost in the South.
LOS ANGELES, Dec. 23.â There was a
heavy frost this morning in exposed
places, but no reports of damage to fruits
have been received. The local weather
bureau to-day sent out a warning notice
to orchardists predicting a heavy frost to
night and advising the. usual precautions
against damage.
BRAZIL, Ind., Dec. 25. â In defending his
mother from an assault by her drunken hus
band, Theodore Watkins, colored. 13 years of
age, shot and killed his father to-night. Wat
kins had attacked his wife with a hatchet.

CONSTITUTIONAL AMENDMENTS.
FOR more than a generation there has been no
amendment to the constitution of the United
States. After adopting such changes in the
fundamental law of the republic as were needed to
settle the issues of the civil war, the people have been
content to carry on the Government under the con
stitution without further alteration. The content
ment, howeve-, has been by no means unanimous.
With each succeeding Congress there have come ef
forts toward amending the constitution and some of
them have received strong support in the House.
This Congress is to be no exception to the rule.
Already several amendments have been suggested,
and some of them may be urged with sufficient force
to become questions of practical politics.
As a matter of course we have a revival of the old
issue of amending the constitution with resptct to
the election of United States Senators. This time,
however, in addition to the plans heretofore proposed
there is a new one advocated by Senator Burrows.
He proposes that when the Legislature of a State
fails to elect a United States Senator the people of
the State shall have z right to elect by direct vote.
The measure is designed to remedy such evils as now
exist in Delaware, which State has no representative
in the Senate by reason of the prolonged Legislative
deadlocks.
An amendment likely to receive considerable favor
is that proposed by Senator Stewart giving the Fed
eral Government authority to control and regulate
for irrigation purposes the waters of rivers flowing
through two or more States. It is believed that un
less some such amendment be adopted it will be im
possible to carry out any comprehensive plan for the
.irrigation of the arid lands lying within the bounda
ries of the States.
The advocates of uniform marriage laws have be
fore Congress an amendment offered by Mr. Gillett
of Massachusetts, providing that Congress shall have
power to establish uniform laws on the subject of di
vorce throughout the United States. The evils re
sulting from the existing differences regarding mar
riage and divorce in the various States have long
been a cause of confusion to lawyers and to courts.
The need of some remedy for them has been repeat
edly pointed out, and the American Bar Association
has made an effort to. bring about the enactment of
something like uniform laws by the State Legisla
tures. Such efforts, however, have effected but little
in the way of reform, and it is therefore not strange
that the more earnest promoters of the movement
should new seek to accomplish it by giving the Fed
eral Government power to act for the whole country.
â , An amendment likely to give rise to much discus
sion is that proposed by Mr. Crumpacker of Indiana,
providing that "Congress shall have power to tax
the capital stock and earnings of all private corpora
tions carried on for the purposes of gain, without re
gard to the population of the States, and such tax
may be progressive." The amendment has a double
object. First it would enable the United States Gov
ernment to levy an income tax on corporations, and
second, by means of such tax it could exert control
over the huge aggregations of capital known as
trusts. The proposed amendment will doubtless be
keenly debated, but there is not much likelihood of
its passage, particularly at this time when the Gov
ernment has a larger revenue than it has any need
for. : -jy
Experience has shown that the people never make
amendments to the constitution for slight causes.' It
took the strain of the civil war to wring consent to
the amendments which grew out of that strife. We
have no strain upon us now, and consequently most
of the proposed amendments will hardly have much
support either in Congress or out of it. When once
the vast work of preserving the forests and conserv
ing the water supply of the West is fairly undertaken
such constitutional amendments as are needed to car
ry it out may be provided, but the repeated failures
of efforts to amend the method of electing United
States Senators seem to prove that the other amend
ments proposed will hardly do more than furnish
subjects for constitutional debates.
