little fight with frost and have won out, and now the
Florida growers have to tackle the same cold propo
sition. .
LACK OF LOCOMOTIVES.
COMPLAINTS made/by the orange growers
of Southern California against the railroads
for an inefficient service in the transportation
of fruit direct attention once more to. an evil which
seems to affect every part of the Union. It appears
the railway managers have been so much occupied
during the .past year in reorganizing, the. roads, and.
manipulating stocks that they have â¢ permitted the
equipments of the reads to run down, and conse
quently have been unable to handle the "traffic- of
fered them by the industries of the country.
Infcie East complaints of the lack of transportation
facilities have been frequent and insistent. Again and
again our exchanges have noted instances of large
factories refusing orders for work solely because they
could not get the railroads to haul their freight. In
one case a large coaling company in Virginia found
itself unable to supply even its regular customers
with coal, and as a "-esult something like a coal fam
ine was threatened over a wide district .of country.
So serious was the evil that it was reported the com
pany thought of applying to the Federal Government
to compel the railroads to furnish cars for its inter
state trade. â¢'â "':. * <A_
Many of our Eastern exchanges after investigating
the trouble have reached the conclusion that the
deficiency is due more to a lack of locomotives than
to a lack of cars. It, is said' that if the railroads had
engines enough to handle their traffic they could in
crease the rapidity of the movement of trains suf
ficiently to make the cars do double the service they
now do. That view seems to be confirmed by the
complaint of the orange growers, who among other
deficiencies of the service allege that in many cases
this season it has taken twenty-five days to transport
a car of oranges to New York and eighteen days to
Chicago, when the distance should have been covered
in less than half the time. It'is clear that if prompt
runs had been made the cars "might have been used'
twice or perhaps thrice in the time consumed in car
rying a single load to market.
It is not likely the roads will be caught next sea
son with so deficient an equipment as that which
they now have. The recent combinations, have prob
ably settled railroad organizations and control for
some years to come, and consequently the managers
will have more time 'to devote to the equipment of
the roads. Furthermore it has been noted that the
orders given for cars and locomotives have this year
been- extraordinarily targe. Next season, then, will
find all the roads with many more cars and loco
motives than they now possess. That fact, however*
will not help the shippers of this year. It therefore
behooves railroad commissions and others having au
thority to do what they can to urge the railroad man
agers to exert their utmost energies to supply the
needs of the time. '
Reports frorJi Great Britain are to the effect that
there would probably have been a congestion of
business along the main railway lines there but for
the adoption of electric trains to handle local trade,
thus leaving the heavy trains free to carry the
through traffic. The use of electric traction has'en
abled the British roads to keep light freights in al
most continuous movement, and a recent estimate
based on the service on the Midland Railroad of Eng
land is said to show that by doing away with infre
quent and heavy trains and maintaining frequent light
trains moving at an average speed of forty miles an
hour foF its entire traffic, the capacity of the road
could be practically doubled.
So successful have these experiments been that
it is believed by some experts that freight movement
by heavy, slow trains will soon be almost entirely
superseded by the continuous movement of light
trains. If that sanguine expectation be fulfilled the
orange growers will not have in the future any com
plaints to make on the score of delay in delivering
fruit to market. Certainly something will have to be
done. A railroad that requires twenty-five days to
haul oranges from California to New York is prac
tically worth very little more to the" fruit trade than
air ox train.
The very fact that the British military authorities
in South Africa are to abandon the concentration
camps is a proof that they have been found too de
structive to human life to be longer defended against
criticism; but the critics will now have a right to ask
why they were not abandoned before so much loss
of life occurred and before the criticisms became so
keen. j}
SUFFRAGE IN THE SOUTH.
TWO resolutions relating to the suppression of
the negro vote in several of the Southern States
are now before the House of Representatives.
One of these is the Crumpacker .resolution, Which
was introduced at the last Congress but not acted
upon. The Other, a resolution by Moody of Massa
chusetts, differs in many respects^from the Crum
packer resolution and is believed to be more likely
to obtain support from the Republican majority.
Either of them, however, will suffice to bring the
Southern arid the negro questions once more before
the country as matters of national politics, and con
sequently there are sure to be serious objections
from all who are opposed to a revival of sectional
issues unless it can be shown that they work an in
jury to the nation as a whole and that a definite and
effective remedy can be provided by Congress.
â¢ The difference^ between the two resolutions is a
wide one. That of Mr. Crumpacker assumes that the
colored population in several of the Southern States
has been suppressed and proceeds to reduce the rep
resentation in Congress from those States in propor
tion to the disfranchisement of the population. ' The
Moody resolution is one of inquiry. It does not ac
cept the validity of the Southern suffrage acts, and
asks an investigation into their constitutionality. If
the acts be found invalid they will^be declared null
and void. ; If they be found to be valid Congress will
be asked to reduce the representation from those
States where disfranchisement prevails, in accord
with the provisions of the fourteenth amendment.
It is said the Southern members of Congress are
not at all afraid of the. Crumpacker bill. They are â 
sanguine it will not be supported by the whole body
of 'Republicans in the House, while it will be opposed
solidly by the Democrats. The Moody resolution,
on the other hand, they regard as dangerous. It will
bring up a on the constitutionality of the
Southern suffrage laws, and on that question not only
would .the "Republicans be united, but many North
ern Democrats would vote with them. In fact, one
Northern Democrat, Mr. Thayer of Massachusetts,
has already said: "The stupidity of the Southern
people in bringing the suppression of the negro vote
so squarely before Congress passes all bounds." He
holds that the Louisiana suffrage law, for example,
is unconstitutional and that the Supreme Court will
so decide. He for one, therefore, will not follow
the Southern lead.
.In the South .the new resolution has excited much
more discussion, than that of Mr. Crumpacker. The
THE SAN FRANCISCO '; CALL; THURSDAY, DECEMBER 26, 1901.
prevailing opinion in that "section seems to be that
it would be better to lose representation in Congress
than to have the suffrage laws declared unconstitu
tional. Georgia, for example, has not disfranchised
negroes and would have nothing to lose in the way
of representation should it be determined to enforce
the provisions of the fourteenth amendment, and yet
the Atlanta Constitution says: "The critics of those
Southern -States, that have adopted the educational
qualification' may be sure of one thing â that those
States will not be driven from their convictions of
What is best by any threatened or actual. reduction of
representation in Congress."
Another view of s the case was presented by a
prominent business man of South Carolina, who,
when asked what he thought of a loss of represen
tation on the part of that State, replied. , "Our repre
sentatives are worth so little it would make no , dif
ference to the State if we lost all."
Whatever view be taken of the question/two
things are certain: First, that the constitutionality
of the various suffrage acts will have to be decided
by the Supreme Court and not by Congress; and*
second, that a. revival of/sectional or racial issues in
this country would be deplorable. Congress, there
fore,, might well wait for the decision of the Supreme
Court. It will then be. time' to act, and the issue
could be settled once and for all without' a prolonged
agitation. * ; 'â ' .
.There has been a scheme in New York to get Cor
nelius Vanderbilt to run against Perry Belmont for
Congress this â¢ fall, but unfortunately it has fallen
through and the boys will not have the flush tinies
they expected when the campaign "opens.
ONCE more a returning Christmas season finds
the peace of the world more seriously dis
turbed by the British and the Boers than by
any other peoples. Our strife in the Philippines and
Russia's aggressions in- Manchuria seem as nothing
in comparison with the struggle in South Africa,
where two civilized aud Christian races are fighting
for supremacy. On each side there are earnest ad
vocates of' peace, but their voices are lost in the
shouts of the / combatants fighting on one side for
empire and on the other for national independence.
Lord Kitchener has not eaten his Christmas dinner
in peace, nor even in comfort. Dewet, Botha and
the other daring .and energetic leaders of the Boers
were never more active than now, and the British
commanders seem at last to have acquired about as
much activity as the Boers themselves. The sur-.
prises of the war are no longer all on one side., The
later reports contain almost as many accounts of
Boer laagers surprised by British troops as of British
outposts surprised by the Boers.
Since the contemptuous rejection of Kitchener's
proclamation calling upon them to surrender there
appears little probability of any yielding upon the
part of theBoers so long as any considerable number
of them are possibly able to keep the field. On the
other hand the- Chesterfield speech of Lord Rose
bery put an end to all hopes that a responsible Brit
ish statesman would undertake the task of restoring
peace by conceding self-government to the men
whom the British seem determined to treat as rebels.
Before Rosebery made his speech a strong section
of the Liberal party had met in convention at Derby
and adopted resolutions favoring the removal of Lord
Milncr frpm South Africa and the concession of vir
tual independence to the Transvaal and Orange Free
State. Had Rosebery taken the same course there
might have been a union of the Liberal party that
could have forced the Ministry to yield at least some
thing for peace. As it is, however, Rosebery remains
a leader without a following and the Liberals a party
without a leader. Practically there is no opposition,
and Chamberlain can go on his course unrestrained.
. It is a doleful Christmas for the men of both
armies. The frequency and the fierceness of the
skirmishes fought during this week show that neither
side feels any relaxation of the spirit of war. The.
fight increases in bitterness and threatens to become
something like a war which will end only by the
extermination of the weaker party. It is another il
lustration to the world that the price of empire is
high, but the strong nations will pay little heed to
the lesson.- It is to be a long time yet before men
will see the realization of peace on earth.
Up to date the claims for damage filed with the
Spanish Claims Commission amount to more than
$6o,ooo,ooo, so the talk about the surplus in the'
treasury may be premature after all.
LESSONS IN SAFE-CRACKING.
ANEW YORK newspaper recently published
an instructive lesson in the art of blowing
. open a safe with nitroglycerine, and the article
has been -promptly lepublished by the evening rail
road organ in this city. The lesson given is easy to
learn, for rot a single detail, of the operation is omit
ted. It explain* to the uninitiated just exactly at
what point a safe can be best attacked, what tools
to use, how to apply them, liow to introduce the
explosive and how to explode it so as. to leave the
safe open to the exploiter. .
The information contained in the article may be
needed by the readers of the New York paper, for
some of them may have been playing the lamb on
Wall street and cannot get their money back without
a resort to burglary. Just why the railroad organ
should republish it, however, â¢ is not . clear, . for the
railroad itself does not have to resOrt to such crude
methods as safe-cracking in order to get other peo
ple's money, and it is not to the interest of the road
to teach the criminal element how to rob the rail
road safes. ;
In all seriousness the publication of such an article
is clearly against public morals. The article is writ
ten in such a way as to tempt persons of criminal ten
dencies to^esort to burglary by teaching them how
easily a safe can be. blown open. It is an instruction
in crime pure and simple. It might serve as a lesson
in a text book in a school for thieves. It will almost
certainly set some criminal to thinking of safe-crack
ing and probably to planning such an exploit. -The
danger, of such crimes is therefore much greater than
it was before the instructive lesson was published.
There is pfSrburse.no way to^ punish a paper that
thus publicly incites criminals to further crime by ex
plaining to.thenr how to commit it with success.
Public sentiment, however, may be able to reach the
editors of journals which publish such things and
convince them that they cannot: expect popular sup
port if they continue such publications. It is not
within the, rightful scope of journalism to teach* men
how to crack a safe any more than to teach them
how to commit murder and escape without detection.

be lekased' and is to come homeland itgoes with
out-saying: the lecture bureaus are ~ getting ready to
do business with , her. . .;

OLYMPIC CLUB PRESENTS SILVER
COLUMN TO JOHN D. SPRECKELS
AN innovation was established at
the Olympic Club yesterday after
noon. Through the efforts of the
board of directors and President
William Greer Harrison a secular
and sacred concert was arranged for the
club members and their lady friends, and
the occasion was taken advantage of to
present to John D. Spreckels a very hand-
Some testimonial of the club's apprecia
tion of a boon which has come from hi*
hands during the last seven years.
The programme, which was entirely mil- j
sical, was under the direction of Wallace j
A. Sabin. In the big gymnasium, dÂ«*co- j
rated with buntings, greens and bunches j
of Christmas berries, the audience gath
ered and listened to the carols of the
choristers of St. Luke's, the splendid |
singing of Donald de V. Graham and Mrs.
M. E. Blanchard,' three solos on the piano
by Miss Huilahand the eloquence of Gen- j
eral Barnes. . On the stage were the offi- j
cers of the club and their, wives, Ciaus
Spreckels, W. G. Irwin. W. D. K. Gttwon,
Joseph Grant, Henry E. Hlghton, Judge
Hebbard, Professor Hengstler and other
prominent guests.
William Greer Harrison In a neatly
turned speech , introduced General
Barnes, who delivered the presentation
address. Unfolding the national colors
from the stiver piece that stood on a ta
ble near the speaker, the gift was ex
posed to view. It is of silver and stands
four feet one inch in height and weighs
325 ounces. I In form it is a graceful Co
rinthian column with . a base of twelve
panels. On one of these panels are em
bossed the arms and emblem of the club,
the remainder portraying athletic exer
cises both ancient and modern. They are
all exquisitely elaborated, showing ath
letes in the quick and action on field and
water. The. base upon which these pan
els rest is marine in character. The work
throughout is â¢ all hand carved. On a
globe surmounting the column is a grace
ful figure of the modern type of athlete.
In his extended arm is a laurel wreath.
Accompanying this handsome piece of
silver is an album containing the auto
graphs, in alphabetical order, of the 1200
donors of the present. The album Is in
itself a work of art. It is bound in leath
er with filigree work of brass, after the
style of the fourteenth century, appliqued
upon it after special designs.
-The inscription on the silver column
reads:
Presented by the Olympic Club to John D.
Spreckels In recognition of his loyal and un
helflsh service In providing for the club Its
splendid salt water system. Christmas day,
1001.
General Barne3 in his presentation
speech told the reason of the gif c To Mr.
Spreckels the: Olympic Club owes its fine
supply of salt water. For seven years the
members of the Post-street athletic insti
tution â¢ have been furnished ', with ocean
water and no charge has ever been made.
The graceful silver column stands as a
testimony of the , Olympic Club's appre
ciation for the kindness and consideration'
of one of Its fellow members.
Presentation by Barnes. . .
The presentation speech of " General
Barnes is as 'follows: '
' Among the interesting events of this Christ
mas commemoration is the public expression
of the grateful esteem of the Olympic Club for
a gentleman whose generous bread has been
cast upon the waters of the Pacific Ocean, and
which, â  after seven years have glided by. he Is
about toÂ« find again in the memorial It Is my

There are many gymnasia and athletic
schools in the United States, but it is uni
versally conceded that of all these Institutions
this is the most complete â the best and the
most comfortable place for work and play in
the whole country, and It Is most of all dis
tinguished by Its equipment of fresh and salt
water for bathing purposes and for the stead
fast culture of the sentiment of personal clean
llneps, which Is the prototype of the higher
civilization. No unclean race was ever a great
race, and he Who regards not his body as the
temple of his soul is not fit to possess the one
cr perpetuate the other.. Four hundred year3
ago Francis Bacon in his great work on the
advancement of learning declared that "clean
ness of body was ever esteemed to proceed from
a due reverence to God." Two hundred years
later the great Methodist preacher. John Wen
ley, admonishing his flock, then, I fear, quite
Inclined to physical self-neglect., if one may
judge by the context of hW discourse, upon
the subject of "dress," uttered the. memorable
epigram which deserves to be engraved in let
ters of gold upon the face of the wall of the
Olympic baths: "Care of the body ia cer
tainly a duty and not a sin;, cleanliness is in
deed next to godliness."
And so, founding the observation upon this
text, we may say that the Olympic Club. If
not a church, is very near one.- and William
Greer Harrison, if not a reverend, is all* the
same revered.
History of the Company.
This splendid structure, devoted to athletic
training is, as I say, especially endowed, and
if only Lord Verulam and the great Methodist
could again cross the boundaries of death and
visit here they might' heartily congratulate
each other -that the" seed th?y sowed had come
to a glorious harvest.
I believe it is now about ten years since
the Olympic Salt Water Company was organ
ized. The. idea of supplying the public with
cheap salt water bathing, easy of access, .was
born of â¢ the -active brain- of William dreer
Harrison. To the capital of the corporation
Messrs. John D. Spreckels and Adolph B.
Spreckels were the, principal subscribers, and
of Its shares they have always retained the
vast majority. This beneficent corporation ex
pended in the construction of its pumping plant'
and connections with the great bath house on
Larkin street more than -$500,000. This, es
tablishment has been an Infinite blessing to
the health of the public who frequent It and
an unmixed good to the neighborhood, whose
sewers Its exhausted waters perpetually flush
The Olympic Club at about the same time, for
certain legal reasons, organized an outside cor
poration and obtained from the Board of Su
pervisors a : salt-water franchise, % whlch It
proposed to transfer* to the Olympic Salt Water
Company in consideration of a perpetual supply
of salt water for bathing purposes. But, un
fortunately, it turned out, upon careful' in
vestigation, that the franchise so offered for
barter carried with It no power to the pur
chaser to collect tolls for any salt water which
might be sold. The agreement between the two
corporations was then clearly void for want
of consideration or, at' least, utterly ineffective
and without value, but that discovery made no
difference to John t>. Spreckels â hla word was
as good as his bond. He had promised the
water to the waiting swimmers of the Olympic
Club and he performed j his promise with re
ligious exactitude, not by way of sale, but by
gracious gift. For more thaa seven years his
, and his associates' pumping plant and pine
lines have the salty, glory of the Pa
cific Ocean and exhaled it* here, in infinite
freshness, with the saline scent of the seaweed
upon it, and in it the vigorous ozone of the
sea. More than 600.000.000 gallons of ocean
water have. In a constant tide that has known
no ebb, beÂ«n delivered into the great bathing
reservoir of your gymnasium to your vast gain
but at a personal loss to Mr. Spreckels and his
brother of more than $100,000. and this costly
benefaction Is assured to you for the future as
long as the salt water company shall exist or
until the bed of the ocean shall be pumped
Cry. ... â¢ . .
It is indeed most fitting" that the Olympic

and clean aports. â¢ To nÂ»e this griff will repre
sent many a pleasant hour of good fellowship
which I have known in the Olympic Club.

ceived. â¢

HIS COMPANION
Beats Him With a Shovel

conscious.
Cornelius P. Williams ai:d Vivian Cow
an, teamsters, employed by Felix Gross,
413 Ninth street, had an altercation yes
terday afternoon in the stable of their
employer, and Williams struck Cowan on
the head with a shovol, inflicting injuries
from which Cowan uitd early this morn
ing.
The men after delivering: a load of coal
Bt the United States Marine Hospital
stopped on the way home at the residence
of Charles Williams, 34 Sheridan street.
There, according to the reports secured
by the police, they had a few glasses of
beer. While the Williamses were drink
ing Cowan drove olT with the team, re
lurr.ing to the barn. Later Williams fol

Words followed, and while they were

into tne loft. He heard the sound of
blows, and coming down found Cowan
lying; cm the floor, bleeding from a num
ber of wounds on the head. Near Cowan
v.as a shovel covered with blood and on
it a quantity of hair. The police were
notified and Cowau was removed to th-2

rested and his name was placed on the

At the Central Emergency Hospital it
was found thac there were four lacerated
wounds on Cowan's head. Dr. Murphy

indication points to a fracture of the
skull. Cowan was placed in bed. He re
mained unconscious and died at 1 o'clock
Williams freely rela-ted the circum
ftanceb attending the difficulty, but

ANGLO-AMERICAN
UNION UNLIKELY
Daily News of London
Takes Issue With
Stead.
LONDON, Dec. 25.â The Daily News this
morning, in an editorial discussing Stead'H
pamphlet on "The American Lion of the
World," admits that Mr. Gladstone's
prophecy that the United States would
replace Great Britain as the premier na
tion has been, on the whole, realized; but
It declares It to be utterly impossible that
Great Britain, as Mr. Stead extravagant
ly expresses it, should enter an Anglo-
American union except after a series of
disasters rendering separate existence im
possible. The paper considers any kind
of an Anglo-American federation equally
unlikely, owing to the Briton's Insatiable
love of caste. For the rest, It confesses
the enormous influence which the United
States wields over British colonies and
that a majority of Irishmen would vote
for federation with the United States.,.' -
The article further says It would be In
teresting to see what must happen If the
I,nlted States should drop its protection
ist policy and should offer to Jamaica, In
dia, or even Australia the immense bribe
of admission on reciprocal terms to their
vast, evtr-extending markets.
Suicide Ends the Love Affair.-
SEATTLE, Wash., Dec. 25.â Dolly
Quappo took a dose of carbolic acid and
died from Its effects this afternoon. She
loved Nell Pickerell, whom she believed
to be a boy. The Pickerell girl mas
querades in men's clothing and has won
the hearts of several susceptible girls.
The Quappe girl was a waitress. She
came here from Portland, where her
mother lives.

LITTLE ROCK, Ark., Dec. 25.â Three

Thompson, a friend of one of the dead

THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, THURSDAY, DECEMBER 26, 1901.
TWO MEN ROB
A LONE WOMAN
Footpads Hold Up Annie
Martin on Powell
- . Street.
Masked Highwaymen Oper
ate in the Shadow of a
Church.
In the shadow of the First Baptist
Church (colored) two masked footpads
perpetrated a daring hold-up on Christ
mas eve. They waylaid and relieved Miss
Annie Martin, who resides at 518 Powell
street, of $38, all the cash that she had
in her purse, and a bank book containing
notes to the amount of 51000. The robbery
was immediately reported to Lieutenant
Birdsall, but the footpads had made their
escape and the police could find nothing
of them. - Â»
When the holdup occurred Miss Martin
was on her way home. The footpads,
both of whom wore masks that concealed
their features, sprang out from' their hid
ing place and confronted the frightened
lady with a pistol. From the deliberate
manner in which the robbers performed
their work the police are of the opinion
that they are professional -footpads. After
having relieved Miss Martin of all the
money which she had, the highwaymen
took to their heels and made good their
escape. At the time of the holdup there
were no people on Powell street to whom
Miss Martin could have appealed for as
sistance. â¢' - * â¢ :
Lieutenant Birdsall reported the hold
up toyCaptain Seymour yesterday and he
detailed two men to search for the foOt
pads. Notwithstanding the fact that the
robbers wore heavy masks, Miss Martin
was able to give a very good description
of them. A notice hag been sent to the
bank in which Miss Martin has her
money deposited instructing the man
agement not to honor the checks that the
robbers secured.
EXPRESSMAN COMMITS
SUICIDE IN A BARN
Charles Steinway Hangs Himself
While Suffering From an At
tack of Melancholia.
Charles Steinway, an expressman whose
headquarters were at the corner of Sut
ter and Stockton street, committed suicide
last night by hanging himself in a stall
at J204 Webster street. He was 45 years
of age. For some time past he has been
complaining and frequently took days off
from his work. He was suffering from
melancholia and drank heavily. Yester
day he complained of being worse.
It is not known at what hour of the
evening Steinway hanged himself. The
body was cut down about midnight by
Special Officer Harvey. The barn in which
the body was found belongs to William
Both. The expressman left a note written
in a foreign language. Â».

The 580 patients and employes at the

arrested and will be held pending the re
sult ol the Coroner's Inquest.
The woman's statement made to Captain
Seymour was In effect that Harrison
knew she had some, dimes and- wanted
them. She had them in her hand and he
made a grab for them. She put them in
her mouth and he struck her on the jaw
and arms. She grabbed him by the lapel
of his coat and threw him. to the ground.
He got up, staggered in the direction of
the .Louisiana lodging house, where they
lived, fell on the steps of a house beforo
he reached there and expired.
The body was removed to the Morguo
and on autopsy allowed that death was
due to tuberculosis, hastened, probably,

woman,'.

formed into a scene of war.
The companies marched out to the scene
of the riot and the .soldiers were then
driven back of the fence that marks the
boundary of the reservation. Here they
collected, still fuming from the engage
ment and their Christmas drinks. On the
other side of the fence the soldiers hoot
ed and yelled and howled. The noise
could be heard for blocks, and many min
utes elapsed before they were dispersed.
Orders were given, out last night that
no one should leave the reservation
whether a pass had been issued or- not.
The turmpil was so great that at a late
hour- last night the residents in the
neighborhood had not yet recovered from

CASBCB0Y BELIEVED OF
A TAILOR-MADE SUIT
Successful Trick Flayed by a Han
by the Use of a Pretended
Message.
R. Itoden, a cash boy employed In J.
O'Brien & Co.'s store, 1146 Market street,
was standing: in front of the store
Wednesday night about 7 o'clock with a
parcel In his possession when a man ap
proached asking him to take a message
to a man named Gordan at the Windsor
Hotel. The man gave the boy 25 cents,
and. taking the parcel from him, -said:
"I'U hold this till you return."
The boy -went to the hotel and delivered
the note. When he returned the man and
the parcel had disappeared. The parcel
contained a lady's black tailor-made suit
and was addressed to Mrs. Davey, 473
Douglass street.

CHRISTMASTIDE SPORTS AND MERRY SOCIAL PASTIMES
MARK THE GAY FESTIVITIES OF THE HOLIDAY SEASON
SAN FRANCISCANS ENJOY AN IDEAL
DAY AT THE CITY'S PLEASURE RESORTS
Park and Ocean Beach Are Crowded With Merrymakers and
Theaters Are Filled to the Doors â Many Residents
Take Advantage of Fine Weather to Visit in the Country
1DKAL weather and prosperity were
the means of making the celebration
of Christmas, 1901, long to be remem
bered by the residents of this city
and vicinity. It was a day of glad
ness. Even the poor were not forgotten,
fts they were invited to partake of a din
ner spread by the Salvation Army.
The tired salesmen and women who
â¢worked incessantly for the past month
were given a much needed holiday. All
the stores were closed, and only places
â¢where refreshments are sold were open.
In the afternoon the various theaters
gave matinee prformances, and the play
houses were crowded. In the evening re
served seats were at a premium. What
with private dinners, entertainments of
all kinds and social affairs the people of
this city spent a delightful day. The
baseball games in the morning and after
noon attracted large crowds. The race
track at Emeryville contained thousands
of visitors, and other sports were liber
ally patronized.
The park was crowded in the afternoon
with pleasure-seekers. Eevery car on
route to the Cliff House was uncomfort
ably filled, and the beach was crowded
with people who enjoyed sunbaths. '
Many people took advantage of the
weather to go into the country and spend
a. quiet Christmas day with their friends.
Trains and boats carried thousands out
cf town. No accidents of note marred the
pleasures of the day.

members of the audience rose from their
seats and heartily and patriotically sang
with the orchestra. It was a spontaneous
outburst, and Mr. Schmitz, touched by
the situation, faced the audience and vig
orously brought voices and orchestra to
gether in rousing harmony. It 'was a
triumph in national chorus. The Mayor
elect telt so and the- audience realized it.
At the conclusion the applause was deaf
ening and the Mayor-elect cannot soon
forget the ovation.
Mr. Schmitz had promised to direct his
own new composition, "The American
Citizen March." He did so, and another
volley of applause awaited him upon his
taking his seat in the conductor's chair
and on the conclusion of the spirited
march. Indeed, so delighted was the
audience with the composition that its
repetition was demanded. The dramatic
part of the programme was given by pu
pils of the Caldwell College of Oratory
and Acting and the Robert Downing In
stitute of Dramatic Art. The play chosen
for the occasion was "The Tigress," in a
prologue and four acts. It was capably
acted and without exception the ladies
and gentlemen taking part showed not
only intelligence, but remarkable dra
matic aptitude.
The following named were prominent
in the drama: Caroline Flanders, Thomas
Graham, Ernst Vincent, Herbert Morris,
Charles Wilson, Louise Saxton. Ethel
Farley, Jack Woolson, Walter Miller, J.
Morris Foster, E. J. Bender. Harriet Jean
Brown, Emily Ross and Master Arden
Saxton.
SCOTS PLAY AT "BOOLS."
Team of Oaklanders Defeats San
Franciscans on Park Green.
Sixteen members of the San Francisco
Scottish Bowling Club had a grimly
merry time for several hours yesterday
playing at the time-honored game of
bowling out on the green sward near the
baseball game ' in Golden Gate Park.
Eight of them were grouped under the
team name of "Oakland," because all
were residents of the town at the other
end of the ferry run. The other .eight
were the "San Franciscos." Two "rinks"
were played and Oakland won them both,
scoring a total of 43 points to San Fran
cisco's 27. San Francisco got " fifteen
points in the first "rink" ana twelve in
the second.
The green is not yet ln condition to suit
the members of the new club, for instead
of the perfect surface they -were used to
on the bowling greens ln the old country
the park lawn presents a succession of
corrugations and little hillocks that keep
the players guessing and interfere sadly
with the most skillful calculation of force
and direction and the amount of "green"
to give the bowl.
But if the sward was not the most sat
isfactory the accent was in the pink of
condition, and many a bowl that appa
rently meant to circle wide of the "jack"
after an erratic course over the green
was coaxed into point-winning position bv
the very richness of the braw Scotch that
the bowler or the leader used to coddle it
The old game is very different from the
one played with so much stage thunder
Jn the alleys. There are no* pins to be
bowled over and no alley to be followed
by the bowl. The green is 12C feet long,
and a white bowl, called the "jack," Ts
rolled across the sward by a team leader.
It must come to rest between the 80- foot
line and the end of the green. Then the
members of teams follow in turn, aiming
to hit the "jack" with their bowls or to
THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, THURSDAY, DECEMBER 26, 1901.
leave them near the "jack," as each bowl
a side has nearer the white one than any
of the opponent's bowls scores a point.
The bowls themselves are built on a plan
to make any one but a Scotchman think
that his Christmas has been too merry.
They are turned with circles on them,
and instead of being perfectly round
there is a bulging portion that makes the
bowl curve after it has rolled a certain
distance across the sward. The curve be
comes greater as the speed decreases, un
til the bowl is making circles somewhere
near the "jack" before it finally comes
to rest. ..
The Oakland team was composed of
Hugh Forgie, Dr. A. T. Crawford. An
struther Dalziel, James Hutchinson,
James Orchison, James P. Taylor, James
Moir and Robert Dalziel. The last named
scored ten points, the highest individual
score.
The San Francisco team consisted of
Joseph Gray, Wallace Cook, John Mc-
Laren, J. C. Moffat, John D. Robertson,
David Dalziel, Andrew Wllkie and An
drew McNair. . ,
Bowling was continued until late in the
afternoon. Among those playing after
the match game were Y. C. Lawson, John
Reid. Robert Park. J. C. Moffat, Andrew
McNair, Joseph Gray and Robert Dal
ziel.
Christinas at the Eoanoke.
Christmas night was spent in a de
lightful fashion at the Roanoke on Bush'
street. After dinner a reception was held
by Mrs. Morris, the proprietor, and her
two charming daughters, after which a
Christmas tree was unveiled and Santa
Claus, in the person of Dick Wise, dis
tributed an appropriate present to each
of the guests. A beautiful, musical pro
gramme was rendered, the principal par
ticipants being Mrs. Laselle, Miss Gilbert
and George H. Richardson.
ZOUR MILLION DOLLARS
A RAILROAD WILL SPEND
Cut-Off Line in the TTorthwest Coun-
try to Be Built Early Next
Year.,
TACOMA, Dec. 25.â The bringing of the
Northern Pacific and Great Northern rail
roads under one control will not prevent
early building by the former of the pro
posed cut-off between Ellensburg and
Llnd, in Central Washington. By this
new line, 110 miles long, the main line
will be shortened ninety-nine miles," be
sides giving a better grade than the pres
ent roundabout line, which runs south
ward to North Yakima and then turns
northward to Ellensburg.
Surveys for the cut-on* were finished
this summer, and surveyors have just
been eent out to finish the work of cross
sectioning it. The traffic of the Northern
Pacific has become so heavy In lumber,
shingles and wheat, that a large saving
can be made on the much shorter haul
which the cut-off will afford.
While the Oregon Railroad and Naviga
tion Company hauls Eastern Washington
wheat down the 1 Cohimbia River water
grade to Portland, the Northern Pacific
is handicapped by its present roundabout
line, together with the necessity of haur
ing grain over the mountains. This is
largely offset by securing its own coal at
Rosslyn at a cost of less than $1 per ton,
while coal costs the Oregon Railroad and
Navigation Company more than double
this price. The advantage will be largely
in the' Northern Pacific's favor after the 1
Lind . cut-off is built. Its cost will be
$4,000,000. Everything indicates that work
will commence early next; spring.
OVERTURNED BOAT TELLS
STORY OF TRAGEDY
Tather and Son Are Believed to Have
Been Drowned Near Santa
Barbara.
SANTA BARBARA, Dec. 25.â In the
channel on comparatively smooth water
a small boat containing Frank -Dowler
and his son Ralph was capsized this aft
ernoon. Both men are supposed to have
been drowned.
Ralph, who was engaged in the oil drill
ing business at Plru.came here to spend
Christmas with his parents, and to-day
he and his father went on a fishing expe
dition to try a new boat which! the father
had recently built. On their non-appear
ance at a late hour this afternoon a
searching party was organized.. The boat
was found bottom-up about three miles
from shore. The bodies have not been re
covered.
BASEBALL STARS
DIVIDE HONORS
Eastern Teams Each Win
on Recreation Park
Diamond.
Nationals Capture Fastest
Game of the Winter
.Series. "
The Nationals and ;the All-Americas
each; won .a game of baseball yesterday
at Recreation Park. The morning game,
which was won by the Nationals by a
score of 3 to 2, was one of the fastest anl
cleverest gaines of the season. It was
witnessed by 200 spectators. In the after
noon 4000 admirers of the game saw the
All-Americas - win by a score of 5 to 2.
The playing did not approach that of the
morning in quality. ..â :â¢. /â & !
Waddell was in the box for the Alt-
Americas in the afternoon and only three
hits were secured off his delivery. He
struck out only two men, which Is away
below his average in previous games. It
is possible he is suffering from "writer's
cramp," as he has '.been, working overtime
recently signing contracts for next sea
son. â  >
"Sandow" Mertes was the hero of the
afternoon game. With two men out in
the third inning he sent the ball over
the center field fence for a home run.
The Nationals scored their second and
lust run In the fourth inning.
Lee, the first man up, was safe at first,
as Davis took: his foot off the -bag in
reaching for the ball. He went to sec
ond on a passed ball and made third on
a wild pitch. He scored on the throw-in
of Steinfeldt's fly out near the flagpole in
center field.
The All-Americas did their scoring in
two innings. In the first they scored one
run. . Umpire McDonald had three close
decisions to render, each r time calling the
runner safe. Beckley and his men stop
ped the game to object to the decisions,
which were not changed. Barrett, the
popular center fielder, was the man who
scored. Pickering, the first man ups hit
to the pitcher -and was safe at fir^t. Bar
rett also hit to pitcher. The ball was
fielded to Mertes at second to shut off
Pickering, but Sandow dropped it. Pick
ering then played too far otf the base and
was put out, Barrett making his way to
second station on the play. Mercer hit
â¢to Mertes and beat the ball to first, Bar
rett going to third. Lajoie hit to second,
forcing Mercer out and bringing Barrett
home. Davis flew out, retiring the side.
The All-Americas bunched tneir hits in
the sixth, sending four men home. Bar
rett led off with a single. Mercer secured
another. Lajoie followed with a clever
sacrifice, advancing his team mates. Da
vis sent the ball out to Carr in right field.
He misjudged the flight of the ball and
Davis was safe at third before it was re
turned to the field. Barrett and Mercer
scored. Irwin's two-bagger scored Davis
and Irwin came home from second on
Sullivan's single.
There were two j unusual plays during
the afternoon. In. the fourth Irwin tried
to reach home from third on a foul fly to
the catcher, but did not succeed.
In the second, with "McHale at the bat
and the bases clear, Waddell commenced
to deliver the ball, but overbalanced him
self and held the ball. The umpire called
it. a "balk," and McHale was given his
There will be no game to-day, but the
same teams will meet again to-morrow
and. Saturday. The score of the after
noon game: -.

Morning Game Is Fast.
The morning game was one of the pret
tiest exhibitions of baseball- seen here
this season. The . Nationals won by a
score of 3 to 2. It was one of the fastest
games of the year, requiring only one
hour and twenty minutes to complete the
nine innings. Schmidt was in the box
for the Nationals and pitched great ball,
showing what he can do with good sup
port. Carr accepted four difficult chances
in right field. .,
Frank Chance, the catcher, was twice
hit by a pitched ball, and each time it
was thought he was disabled. The score:

RIO SHANNON, A 15 TO 1 OUTSIDER,
TAKES THE CHRISTMAS HANDICAP
Jockey Lee Jackson Gives the Long Shot a Perfect Ride,
Beating Vesuvian Handily at the Finishâ Entries of
(3reen Morris Out of Luckâ Holiday Crowd Hit Books
RIO SHANNON, piloted by Lee
Jackson, and 15 to 1 in the bet
ting; furnished the sensational
feature : at Oakland yesterday by
capturing the rich Christmas
handicap. - Favorites divided honors with
long shots, , the' twenty-four books were
bombarded and battered out of all shape,
and what' more could an immense holiday
gathering wish for? When Sidney Bender,
the plunging talker,, told his friends in
New Orleans he found no sunshine in Cal
ifornia while here, he must have resided
in a basement and labored at night. More
perfect weather could not be found. Many
of the ladies were attired in summer silks,
and only those of, the male persuasion
whose systems had become^debilitated by
indulging in ferry-boat diet resorted to
topcoats. In the ring the crowd was so
dense that it was more difficult for a
stranger to place a bet than to pick up
a "sleeper" in a Kentucky faro' game.
Unfortunately for the layers of' odds,
though, the three long-shot winners, Sam
Green, Rio Shannon and Magi, were so
strongly played â¢ that nothing was laid
away. Even the field books retired from
action with severe casualties. As a rule
their position is considered as invulnera
ble as was the Boers' on Spion Kop.
The wonderment of the spectators was
the staying qualities exhibited by Rio
Shannon in the $3000 Christmas handicap,
at one and a quarter, for which nine faced
the webbing. Green Morris started three â
Watercure 128, Autolight 122 and Janice
with 105 pounds up. Against this trio of
smart ones 7 to 10 could be had. To an
uneven start the chances of Autolight,
Janice and Brutal were shattered. Icicle,
from P. Ryan's barn, ridden by Tommy
Burns and well respected in the ring,
cut quite a figure in the lead for a mile;
then the summer sun got in its work.
After Icicle had undergone the evapora
tion process Lee Jackson, astride Rio
Shannon, moved from second to first po
sition, and, while Vesuvian once looked
troublesome, "Jimmy" Coffey's buggy
horse paraded past the judges two lengths
in front in 2:06 flat. Johnny Woods,
with Watercure, unloaded his cargo in
third place, followed closely by Auto
light, well kicked and thumped by Win
nie O'Connor. But for his piece of ill
luck at the start the last named- horse
would undoubtedly have earned more
than the bones of the turkey .to pick
on. Greyfeld was also unfortunate,
Mounce getting cut off on the back
stretch while endeavoring to go through
on the rail. '
Jockey. Owen Mounce landed Parlzade
first in the opening two-year-old scramble
by a head from Royal Rogue, a 15 to 1
shot. The' St. Carlo filly outbroke her
field and led throughout. Flourish, with
Bullman up, took show honors. Snowber
ry was nearly put over the fence on the
far turn by Royal Rogue.
For a- time. during the progress of the
six and a half furlong run it looked as if
Gibraltar might get the purse. With Ed
die. Jones in the saddle he led to the
stretch, when he suddenly decided to sulk,
running unplaced. Never far out of it,
Wyoming, the favorite, worked 'his way
to the front and driving hard got the
verdict from Orleans by a head. King
Dellis had a stormy trip.
A surprise .was furnished in the hurdle
event, over a mile and three-eighths, for
which Phil Archibald and Rainier, coupled
as a stable, ruled pronounced favorites.
Phil Archibald, the mainstay of the en
try, piloted by "Stew" Peters, came to
grief at the second jump, his rider escap
ing Injury. Distance, a 20 to 1 shot and
a handy jumper, looked to have the race
won, when his rider eased up and was
led out more than a length by Sam Green,
a 15 to 1 shot, ridden by Evans. Lord
Chesterfield got away from the post poor
ly, never showing prominently.
Another member of the Jennings string
finished second in the San Leandro handi
cap, at six furlongs, for two-year-olds.
Sister Jeanie, astride which O'Connor sat,
went to the post a 3 to 2 choice, and in a
long drive had her colors lowered by
Magi, a 10 to 1 shot played to 7. Escalante
quit after showing first to the stretch.
Bullman on the 11 to 5 favorite Cougar
captured the final mire selling run. At
the close he was hard pressed by the two
year-old Bragg, ridden by Knapp, win
ning by a neck. only.
Track Notes.
Twenty-one books did business In the
ring, with three field books to accommo
date the more conservative players."
Up to yesterday George Rose was cred
ited with being $17,000 winner since he cut
in, only a short while back. He lost about
$2000 yesterday. . ' . â 
R. B. Milroy, who for years has been
connected with the association across the
bay, was well remembered by the horse
men. His Christmas box came in the
form of a magnificent case of silverware
and a very handsome dressing case, the
latter being presented to Mr. and Mrs. R.
B. Milroy.
Eddie Greaney was fortunate enough to
have a good bet down, straight and show,
on Rio Shannon.
GENERAL AXGEB SAFELY
PASSES CRITICAL PERIOD
DETROIT, Mich., Dec. 25.â Dr. C: G.
Jennings, who 13 attending General R.A.
Alger, said to-night: %
You may announce that General Alger has
safely passed the first and most critical stage
after the operation that was performed on him
last Sunday. Of course there will be a pos
sibility of dangerous developments for at least
another week, but we do not expect any.
The bulletin on the general's condition
issued to-night says: â¢ Â«
General Alger passed a very comfortable day.
The patient has had no fever; his pulse has
ranged from 68 to 82 throughout the day. He
will be given liquid nourishment by the stomach
for the first time to-night. I It will be a solu
tion of egg albumen. The drainage from the
gall bladder is perfect. C. G. JENNINGS.
Forr the first time since the operation
all the family were to-day admitted to
General Alger's room. The general was
very bright and cheerful throughout the
day, and was ambitious to do more and
see more people than his physicians would
permit. â

Last Words at Grave
of Daughter.
Actress* Final Resting Place
Is on Hillside Over-
looking Bay.
Oakland Office' San Francisco Call.
1US Broadway, Dec. 25.
Joaquin Miller, the poet of the Sierras,
this afternoon said the last sad rites for
S?' h'M,?* the srave of hls daughter.
Maud Miller, the actress. The old man.
1S ,^ erâ¬ft Â° r aU his kin but one-the
child of his daughter-stood in the breeze
which blew his white hair about, and ad
dressed a few parting words to the dead
clay of his offspringâ a sight that moved
the spectators to tears. *~
Maude Miller's grave lies on a hillside
overlooking the bay and its environs, in
a spot where a number of Joaquin Mil
vfÂ«T h,/^ nds are buried and where he will
be buried in time. A mass of stones marks
the spot where the poet wishes his re
mams to rest. His daughter's grave is
near it. There were not many people in
attendance upon the obsequiesâ only a
lew of the more intimate friends of the
lamily and some of the deceased's asso
ciates. The remains were first taken to
the poet's home on the heights, and then
Dome by the pallbearers a quarter of a
mile to the spot that was marked by a
newly opened grave.

EELIANCE CLTJB OFFEBS
ATTRACTIVE PROGRAMME
Hank Griffin and Jack Johnson Will
Contribute Main Event of the
Tournament.
OAKLAND, Dec. 25.â The Reliance Club
offers an attractive programme for its
regular monthly boxir.gr tournament, to be
held on Friday evening at the club gym
nasium.
The main event promises to be of more
than ordinary interest. Jack Johnson, the
Eastern heavy-weight, has been matched
to meet Hank Griffin of Los Angeles. Both
lighters are colored and each has some
what of a reputation for administering
and receiving punishment. They have met
before, and in Bakersfield went twenty
fast rounds, with honors about evenly di
vided, although Griffin got the decision.
Griffin a few weeks ago humbled the
"pride of the British navy," as Con Shee
l^an calls himself, and then proved him
self a lighting man of considerable ac
count. Both men are reported to be in
first-class condition.

HATU'ARDS, Dec. 25.â A number of

Five Men Hurt in a Fight.
OAKLAND, Dec. 23.â In a free-for-all
fight on Park avenue, Emeryville, five
racetrack hangers-on were so badly cut
and beaten that for three hours the Re
ceiving Hospital staff was busy sewing
and patching wounds. Vance Powell,
colored, was seriously injured. Hia skull
was fractured and his head was a mass
of bruises. Arthur Mitchell, white, was
stabbed in the right thigh. Walter Mc-
Neary. colored, had his forehead cut open
to the skull, and George Webster, colored,
had his scalp laid open with a cut three
inches long. A white man known as
"Texas" was cut and bruised. He was
sent to the County Jail. The fight oc
curred at 9 o'clock and was the result of
holiday drinking.

BOER TRAITORS
SERVE ENGLAND
Captured Burghers Are
Said to Be Assisting
Kitchener.
New Commandos Composed
of Former Patriots Will
Be Formed.
PRETORIA. Dec. 23.â The formation of
a new burgher corps, to be known as
National Scouts, has aroused' great in
terest among the surrendered burghers.
In view of numerous meetings and appli
cations General Lord Kitchener has au
thorized the creation of fresh wings to
operate in the Southern and Eastern
Tranvaal under permanent Boer leaders.
The forces are to be composed of burgh
ers only.
4t is said that the recent success of the
British is due to the co-operation of two
burgher commandos acting under Gen
eral Bruce Hamilton. These commandos
include many captured Boers.
STENOGRAPHERS WANTED
FOB, PHILIPPINE SERVICE
Chance for Young Men to Enter the
Government's Employ in
Manila.
WASHINGTON'. Dec. 25.â The Civil
Service Commission yesterday issued no
tice of a special examination to be held
on the 6th and 20th of January, 1902, for
positions of stenographers and typewrit
ers in the Philippines, as the list of eli
gibles has become reduced and the Ma
nila authorities have sent notice that they
need twenty or more to rill existing va
cancies. The examinations will be held
in Washington. Boston. Chicago, Cincin
nati, Denver, New Orleans, New York,
San Francisco and Seattle. Appointees
will receive a salary of $1200 per annum,
those who prove competent to be pro
moted to $1400 per annum in six months
or less time if vacancies occur subsequent
to the date of appointment. Applicants
will be required to pass a physical exam
ination, and .appointees will be required
to remain in the service at least two
years. The applicants must be between
the ages of IS and 40 years.
This examination offers an excellent
opportunity to young men to enter a
service which has many attractive fea
tures and to see a most interesting part
of the world.

THE DAY'S DEAD.
SAN JOSE, Dec. 25.â Mrs. Elizabeth
Cavins Rhodes, wife of Superior Judge
A. L. Rhodes, formerly of the California
Supreme bench, died this morning of
pneumonia. She was a native of Green
County, Indiana, and in her seventy-third
year. She had resided with her husband
in this city since 1856 and continuously in
the house where she died for nearly forty
six years. Besides her husband four chil
dren survive her, Mrs. Mary Barstow,
James H. and E. L. Rhodes of this city
and Sam R. Rhodes of Hermosillo. Mex
ico. Judge and Mrs. Rhodes celebrated
their golden wedding five years ago. She
was a woman of great energy and marked
Intellectuality, and Judge Rhodes has al
ways attributed to her the credit for
much of hiÂ« success in life.
DE WITT CXINTON HOUSE.
Oldest Traveling Man in United
States Dies at Kansas City.
KANSAS CITY. Dec. 25.â De Witt Clin
ton House, who is dead at his home in
this city, aged 74 years, was one of the
oldest and best known traveling men in
the United States. He was born in Ver
mont, and fifty years ago established at
Peoria the first steam bakery and crack
er factory in Illinois. When the War of
the Rebellion broke out House went to
Minnesota, enlisting in St. Paul In the
Sixth Minnesota Volunteers. Shortly
after his enlistment his regiment was sent
to subdue and punish the Sioux Indians
under Chief Little Crow, who had at
tacked settlers in Western Minnesota and
killed many people in the massacre of
New Ulm. While engaged in burying the
dead settlers House and about forty other
soldiers were corralled by a band of In
dians at Birchacoola. After a heroic de
fense of four days, during which time
most of the little band were either killed
or wounded, the survivors were rescued
by the other troops. At this battle House
received a severe wound in the left leg.
Alexander R. Andrews.
REDDING. Dec. 25. â Alexander R.
Andrews, a pioneer of this county, died
this morning. He was a native of Martha
Mills, Kentucky, and 73 years of age.
Heart trouble was the cause of his death.
Andrews was a veteran of the Mexican
War and a Mason of high standing. In
early days he represented the district in
the State Assembly and filled numerous
county officers since. He leaves a wife
and two daughters. The daughters are
Mrs. Edward Sweeny, wife of the Su
perior Judge of this county and Mrs. W.
J. Gillespie.

neral arrangements will be announced

SUIT FOR LIBEL
WITH CROKER AS
THE DEFENDANT
Perry Belmont Resents In
s'nuation That He Used
Money to Obtain
Nomination.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
NEW YORK, Dec. 25.â Perry Belmont
has instructed his attorneys to bring suit
against Richard Croker for libel.
"Let him go ahead and sue," says Cro
ker.
All this is the result of Croker's unfort
unate absence in. Indiana when Seventh
District Democrats were trying to nomi
nate a man for Congress. They have nom
inated four men, Tammany contributing
twoâ Mr. Belmont and Councilman
O'Grady. The Republicans are content
with one. Montague Lesler.
Belmont said on Tuesday that Croker
did not tell the truth in saying he had not
interfered in the selection of a candidate.
i Belmont added that he himself was not
Croker's candidate and did not want to
be.
Croker rejoined to-day as follows:
v "All I have to say is that the only in
terfering I did was for Belmont's own
good. If somebody had not interfered the
Grand Jury would have interfered."
Upon reading these words in the even
ing papers to-day Belmont replied;
"Croker's statement that he had inter
fered for my good and that if he had not
interfered the Grand Jury would is of
such a character that it requires a posi
tive, unmistakable answer. He says I ob
tained my nomination by the use of
money. On account-of that statement I
have decided to bring suit for libel."
STREET CAR STRIKES
THE DONORS OF GIFTS
Agents of the Volunteers of America
Severely Injured in San Jose
Collision.
SAN JOSE, Dec. 25.â A. W. Kennedy and
William Kidd. while engaged in distrib
uting Christmas boxes to the poor of the
city, on behalf of the 'Volunteers of
America, received serious injuries this
morning. , â 
The wagon in which the men were driv
ing was struck by a street car. Kennedy's
spine was badly injured, but the full re
sult cannot yet be determined. Kidd suf
fered a complicated . dislocation of the
shoulder and many severe cuts and
bruises. Both men say the car was driven
at reckless speed and that no bell was
rung for the crossing. The Christmas sup
plies were badly scattered and to a con
siderable extent rendered valueless. t
Prominent Pomona Man Dying.
POMONA, Dec. 25.â James Beckett, one
of the foremost citizens of Pomona Val
ley, is dying at the Pomona Athletic Club.
He was stricken there with apoplexy this
afternoon after having his Christmas dirf
ner. He became unconscious and physi
cians said he had no chance of recovery.
Beckett organized the Consolidated Water
Company of this city and amassed a large
fortune in the investment. He is a cousin
of Secretary of Agriculture Wilson.

PAWNEES YEARN
FOR WAR AFTER
EATING BUFFALO
Taste of the Game Arouses
Oklahoma Redskins and
â Ranchers Flee to, the
Towns.
GUTHRIE, Okla., Dec. 25.â The Paw
nee Indians are on the warpath. An im
mense buffalo bull was so injured in be
ing transferred from the Santa Fe stock
yards to "Pawnee Bill's ranch south of
town that he had to be killed. The meat
was given by Major Lillie to the Indians
for a buffalo dance. The Indians have
been dancing and powwowing ever since.
They claim the vast herds of buffalo are
coming back to this country and many of
the Indians have notified the white les
sees to vacate their ranches at once, as
they wish to lay the fences low so that
the buffalo will have full sway of the
country. â¢ .
The white in the remote parts'of
the reservation are coming into Pawnee
and report the Indians are acting in a
threatening manner toward them. Paw
nee Bill has placed mounted guards
around his buffalo ranch, as the Indians
are camping in the timber west of there
and in sight of the herd. He believes
they Intend to liberate the buffaloes and
kill them.
The United States Indian Agent has
been notified and has made a report to
the Commissioner of Indian Affairs in
Washington.

Banker Meets Peculiar Death.
AKRON, Ohio, Dec. 25.â D. P. Wheeler,
cashier of the Citizens' National Bank,*
was found dead in front of the vault in
the bank this afternoon. , He -had been
killed by an electric shock Uhrough hand
ling a socket of an incandescent lamp and
opening the vault door at the same time,
thus forming a fatal current.

BERKELEY WINS
IN THE SOUTH
University Football Men
Too Fast for the
Angelenos.
Scores Six Touchdowns and
"Safety" to Opponent's
Nothing.
-LOS ANGELES, Dec. 25.â The Uni
versity of â¢' Calif orna football eleven
smashed and battered the heavy line of
the local All Southern California team
this afternoon for six touchdowns and a
safety, scoring in all 38 points, without al
lowing the Los Angeles players a single
chance at the goal line. The game was an
easy one for the college men and was
only saved from being a farce by the
fast work and clever team play of the
Berkeley eleven. Outweighing the .varsity
team by fifteen pounds to the man,
the All Southern Calif ornia team was
supposed to be in fit condition to hold
its opponents down to a lower score, but
the team did not succeed in making five
yards once during the game nor in pre
venting Berkeley from keeping the ball
for consecutive downs.
, Only a fair crowd was at Fiesta Park
to witness thq contest, which was played
under a clear sky ..and blazing sun. Ba
zata, for the All Southern Calif ornias,
kicked off to Womble, who .ran the ball
In for twenty-five yards. After' a series
of small gains by the college backs Over
all punted and Los Angeles returned after
attempting to make the distance. Berke
ley worked the ball to the locals' five
yard line, where Womble fumbled and
lost it. Bazata's punt was blocked and
the ball rolled over the line, scoring a
safety for the varsity team. The kick
out was received by Duden, who fumbled
and recovered instantly. Then by long
gains around the end by Womble and a
thirty-yard run by Mini with the assist
ance of a "backs-over" formation, Berke
ley scored the first touchdown. Overall
kicked the goal and the score stood 8 to
0. Two more touchdowns followed in
quick succession, the "University men dis
playing perfect team work and ability at
line smashing. All Southern California
was forced to call in substitutes twice
during the half to replace injured play
ers. At the end of the first twenty-five
minute half the score was 20 to 0 in favor
of Berkeley. *
Substituting Whipple for Duden at full
and Hendricks for Overall at guard,
Berkeley crossed the line three more times
in the second half. In the middle of the
half Mini was disabled and gave way to
Hudson, whose place at end was filled by
Williams. The local- team was clearly
outclassed and entirely unable to with
stand the fast play " of the varsity.' Ba
zata was outpointed 'by More and Over
all and lost ground every kick. â¢ With
splendid interference Albertson, Â«cored
the last touchdown, which defeated the
local team by a total of 38 points. Fol

The Michigan football giants availed of

CELEBRATE CHRISTMAS
BERKELEY, Dec. 25.â Special masses

vested choir:
Processional hymn, "Come. O Ye Faith
ful"; Introit anthem, "Nazareth" (Gou
nod); "Credo" (Dykes); communion serv
ice hymn, "Hark, the Herald Angels

Love Begotten," old tune: "Gloria in Ex
celsis" (Dykes); recessional hymn, "While
the Shepherds Watch Their Flocks," old
tune. ,
The church had been artistically dec
orated by W. A. Powell ana Ivan M. L4n
forth. In front of the chancel was a
screen in . evergreen, surmounted by
candles. Over the altar was an arch, of
red candles. The body of the church was
decorated in evergreens interspersed with
oak.
Nine masses were celebrated at St. Jo
seph's Church. At the 8 o'clock mass in
strumental music was rendered by the
children of St. Joseph's School. The chil
dren's mass proper was celebrated at 9:15
o'clock, when Christmas carols were sung
by the little folk. The music was pro
duced under the direction of the sisters
of the school and the choir, composed of
the sisters, sang at the other masses.
High solemn mass was celebrated at 10:13
o'clock by the Rev. Father Cantwell. The
Rev. Father Dornan preached and the
Rev. Father Riordan acted as sub-dea
con.

WITHNEY GAINS
TENNIS HONORS
Champion Defeats All
Comers in Singles
Tournament.
Smith Loses a Game by
Default* Through an
Error.
Champion George F. Whitney carried oft
premier honors in the Christmas class sin
gles tennis tournament which was decided
yesterday on the courts of the California
Club. He was easily the best player
among the forty who particpated in the
tournament. The handicaps favored the
championship class. The odds allowed by
the championship and first-class players
to the lower classes were practically the
same. -In the last tournament R. N.
Whitney, winner, of the championship
class, allowed Fred Brown, winner of the
first class, a handicap of "fifteen" and
won handily. Yesterday George Whitney
allowed the first class winner but "four
sixths of nfteen." He won easily from
Collier in the final.
The feature of the day was W. B. Col
lier's defeat of Grant Smith in the first
class. It was thought Smith, after the
excellent showing of last Sunday, â¢ would
win in his class easily, but he was decid
edly "off" in his game and proved easy
for Collier. The latter made few errors
and played a. remarkably steady â  game.
,The score in his favor was 6-1, 6-4.
In the second class Chet Smith carried
off the honors. He won both his matches
yesterday wthout the loss of a set. He
beat Frost 6-4, 7-5, and Homer Parker
6-1, 6-3. .
Herbert Schmidt won the third class on
Sunday. Fourth class honors went to
Leonard Wood, who played a long match
with Al Kenyon in the finals. The score
in Wood's favor was 6-3, 4-6, 7-5.
The championship class match between
R. N. and G. F. Whitney was not played
out. It was agreed between the brothers
that George should play in the interclass
tournament.
In the play-off the draw resulted as fol
lows: L. E. Wood vs. Chet Smith, Col
lier a bye, G. F. Whitney vs. H.
Schmidt. Chet Smith defaulted through
a misunderstanding. The committee de
cided the matches should go over until
next Sunday and Smith left the grounds
with that understanding. Shortly after
it was decided to play the matches off
yesterday and Smith not being present
the game went by default. â¢â¢
The best handicapping was that in the
match between Collier and Wood. The
odds in Wood's favor were "thirty and
two-sixths," and proved right. Collier
won 6-0, 7-9, 7-5. Whitney allowed Schmidt
thirty and won without an effort, the
score beine 6-2. 6-0. j
In the final Whitney allowed Collier odds
of "four sixths of fifteen" and won in
straight sets, 6-1, 6-2, 7-5.

CULLOM'S BILL
EXCITES MIRTH
AND RIDICULE
Americans in London Rail at
the Proposal to Send a
Delegation to the
Coronation.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
LONDON, Dec. 25.â A Central News dis
patch informs amused Americans here
that Mr. Cullom is introducing a bill in
the Senate providing for a Congressional
delegation of twelve to attend King Ed
ward's coronation as representatives of
the United States.
The absurdity of this proposition is
equaled by its snobbery. Such a delega
tion would be a source of unspeakable
embarrassment to the court.
The Earl Marshal, the Duke of Norfolk,
cannot now find places for those subjects
of the King and the Embassadors who
have a right to appear at all the ceremo
nies. What would he do with a junket
ing party of twelve thrust so unexpect
edly on his hands? >

â¢ Patti Helps Out of a Dilemma.
Last winter Madame Patti was staying
for a few days in an isolated village at
the extreme end of Yorkshire. To kill the
monotony of the place the prima donna
went one night to a concert given in aid
of a certain village institution. Not half
of th<* performers turned up. Appreciat
ing the difficulty, Madame Pattiâincog
nita, of courseâ offered to oblige the audi
ence with a song or two. Then she sang,
in her own glorious way, three of her
sweetest. ballads. At the close the chair
man approached and in solemn tone
thanked her. "Well, miss." he said,
"you've done uncommon well. And al
though 'Arry Ock. the juggler, who thinks
nowt of takin' 'old of 'ot pokers and
a-swaliorin* needles, couldn't turn up, yet
you've pleased us very considerably,
miss."
Golf Unmakes a Minister.
A, Scotch "meenlster" took up golf. and.
despite great practice, could not succeed
in passing the tyro stage. His simple ex
clamations of "Tut, tut!" "Oh, dear,
now!" "Well, well!" and the like were
plain evidences of a perturbed spirit. One
day, when the perspiration flowed freely
from his brow and his honest contenance
shone with radiance, which was not due
to calmness of soul but rather to the heat
of the sun and his laborious efforts to
move the obstinate gutta percha from Its
station on the tee, he was tempted to in
dulge in strong language. "Dear, dear,
but I'll have to gie it up, I'll have to gie
it up!" he said at last, with a despairing
look at the ball. "Give up the game?"
exclaimed his friend, who had been a wit
ness of his attempts. "Na, na, the meen
istry," answered the other, with a sl^h.'

ia dead and one of the negroes is dying.

THE California Debris Commission, having re-
ceived applications to mine by the hydraulic
process from Jerry Buckley and A. H. Soper,
In the Gray Lizard mine. In Butte County,
near Rackerby, to deposit tailings In Swedes
Flat Creek; from Joseph D. Countryman, in
the Tannery mine, at Oreson Hill. Yuba
County, to deposit tailings In Oregon Creek;
ana from Manuel Fernandez, in the Inde-
pendence mine, at New York Flat. Yuba
County, to deposit tailings behind the dam
of the Nevada Mining Company, gives notice
that a meeting will be held at room 59,
Flood building. Ean Francisco, Cal.. on Jan-
nary 6, 1902, at 1:30 p. m.
THE annual meeting of the stockholders of The
Nevada National Bank of San Francisco.
Cal., will be held at Its banking-house, 301
Montromery et., San Francisco. Cal., on
TUESDAY. January 14. 1002. at 3:15 o'clock
p. tn.. for the election of directors to serve
for the ensuing year and the transaction of
ruch other business as may come before the
meeting. GEORGE GRANT. Secretary.

WEATHER CONDITIONS AND GENERAL
FORECAST.
A storm of moderate energy is central over
British Columbia, and rain has fallen over
Washington and Northwestern Oregon. Else
where on the Pacific Slope fair weather pre
vails. The pressure has fallen over Oregon
and Washington and risen over California and
the plateau region.
The temperature has risen along the coast
of Southern California and remained nearly
stationary in other districts.
Forecast made at San Francisco for thirty
hours ending midnight, December 26. 1001:
Northern California â Fair Thursday, except
cloudy in the extreme northern portion; light
variable winds.
Southern California â Fair Thursday; light
northerly wind.
Nevada â Cloudy In north portion; fair In
south portion Thursday; warmer In north por
tion; Ught southerly wind.
fcan Francisco and vicinity â Fair Thursday;
light northeasterly wind.
G. H. WILLSON.

the early morning tides are given In the left
hand coiutnn and the successive tides of the

SHIPS ARE GAY
WITH BUNTING
Flags and Evergreens
Us9d in Decoration
of Ships.
Masters of Vessels in Fort Join
in the Celebration of
the Holiday.
Christmas day was not celebrated in a
very ostentatious manner along the water
front. Many of the vessels in the harbor
had branches of evergreen at the mast
head and a few of them were decorated
with signal flags. The vessels noticeable
by reason of- their decorations were the
Sonoma and Lady Wentworth and the
transports Sheridan and Thomas.
Three hundred apprentices from the
English vessels in the harbor sat down to
a dinner given by the Seamen's Institute
at the hall on East street yesterday. They
spent several hours in disposing of all the
good cheer and edibles provided by the
institute, and returned to their various
ships grateful and pleased.
Boatman Harry Johnson rescued two
men from the waters of the bay early
yesterday morning. They are Joe Ander
son and Charles Christiansen, sailors.
They got .into an argument over their
swimming abilities, with the result that a
wager was made that Anderson could not
beat Christiansen swimming to Goat Isl
and. The men started on the race, but
the water was cold and becoming fright
ened they shouted for help. Johnson
heard their cries, put off in a boat and
pulled them out of the water twenty feet
from the end of Folsom-street pier, their
starting point.
The steamer Sonoma is scheduled to sail
for Sydney via Honolulu to-day at 10
a.m. She will leave on time if the colo
nial mails are not delayed.
The captain of the American ship Aryan
has not a very good memory, and to hia
forgetfulness is due the prevention of his
ship leaving port. The shipping laws pro
vide that all vessels shall be inspected at
least'- once a year. The Aryan's captain
forgot that more than a year had elapsed
since his ship had been inspected, and in
consequence when he trie'd to clear yes
terday he was notified that his ship could
not leave port until the local inspectors
had passed 1 upon her serviceability. As
the Aryan is loaded with grain for the
United Kingdom the captain's lack of
memory is likely to prove costly.
The America Maru Is the next vessel
due to arrive from the Orient. The Aus
tralia, from Tahiti, is due here any day.
The only deep water vessel to leave the
harbor yesterday was the big schooner
Helene. She sailed for Honolulu.
Andrew Sivertsan was treated at the
Harbor Hospital yesterday for burns in
flicted by carbolic acid. He claimed that
the fluid had been thrown into his face
by a jealous woman.
Shipping Intelligence.

SUFFERS WHILE
VESSEL DRIFTS
Fireman John Henery of
the Gualala Scalded
by Steam.
Bursty Pipe the Cause of
Serious Accident on
a Schooner.
John Henery, a fireman on the steam
lumber Bchooner Gualala, swathed in
bandages, suffering untold agony anu
hoping and praying that death would
come and end his pain, was brought
ashore from the vessel named yesterday
at noon, shortly after it . had
been towed Into port by the steamer Na
tional City. Blistered from head to foot
It hardly seemed possible that he would
ever reach land, *ut reach it he did anil
he is now occupying a cot at the Marine
Hospital.
John Henery's experience on the voyage
of the Gualala which ended yesterday is
one that he will long remember If he sur
vives. An iron steam pipe burst in the
engine room of the vessel and for three
minutes the unfortunate iireman was un
der the steam. That he was able to es
cape with his life is due to the bravery of
Assistant Engineer Houston of the Gua
lala.
Overcome by the Steam.
The p*pe burst Tuesday night about 7
o'clock, when Henery was the only man
of the engineer's force on duty. The
Gualala was carrying a full head of steam
at Ihe time, and in a moment the engine
room, which is a small one, was filled with
the vapor. With the bursting of the pipe
came the darkness, for the lack of steam
caused the shutting down of the dynamo
which lighted the ship. Henery, blinded,
made a dash for the companlonway lead
ing, from the engine room to the upper
deck. He was confused, ran into a
Btanchion and was thrown to the floor.
Regaining his feet he tried again, but his
scalded, blistered frame was not equal
to the task and he^fell once more to the
floor, and this time he remained where he
fell. His shipmates, who had been awak
ened by the explosion and the hissing
sound of the escaping steam, were crowd
ed about the hatchway leading to the
room where the explosion occurred,
shouting and yelling directions to the un
fortunate fireman. They received only
groans in reply. ,
Houston to the Rescue.
Finally Houston dashed down the steps
which Henery had been seeking blindly
and holding on to the rail swept his
hands about him. They came in contact
with Henery's prostrate form and in a
moment the fireman was dragged Into the
air, but so blistered that he was scarce
recognisable. Then came a time of suffer
ing for the unfortunate man. The vessel,
bereft of power to stem the wind and cur
rent, drifted about helplessly. For hours
the craft was at the mercy of wind and
wave, and during all this time Henery,
despite all the efforts of his shipmates,
suffered intensely.
At 3 a. m. yesterday, when the Gualala
was fifteen miles south of Bowens Land
ing, the steamer National City was sight
ed, and a blue rocket summoned her to the
aid of the lumber schooner.
All possible speed was made by the
National City after she had taken the
schooner in tow and at noon yesterday
she reached port. Henery was taken to
the Harbor Hospital, where his blistered
body was dressed. He was then sent to
the Marine Hospital. He is terribly
burned and it is not expected that he will
recover. Henery i3 a single man and re
sides at 308 Folsom street.
Traded Salmon for Beer.
George McLaughlin was arrested yes
terday on a warrant charging him with
petty larceny. He is accused along with
others of stealing nine cases of salmon
from one of McNab & Smith's trucks on
Brannan street, near Zoe, Monday. Three
of the cases were recovered from a saloon
on Brannan street, where it is said Mc-
Laughlin exchange them for beer.

BOILERMAKER ENDS HIS DEBAUCH
BY SERIOUSLY WOUNDING TWO MEN
Earl G. Westmore, who claims to be a
boiler-maker at the Risdon Iron Works,
terminated his Christmas carousal last
right by shooting and seriously injuring
two Inoffensive men. Hans Halversen,
one of the wounded men, is shot through
the groin and has but a slight chance of
recovery. He Is night clerk in the Mont
gomery Hotel, 227 Second street, and ha
was shot by Westmore while tending to
his dutips. Charles Penne, Westmore's
second victim. i.Â« a barkeeper in Captain
Ubemeyt-rs saloon at 620 Alarket street.
He was shot through the left thigh while
walking along Tehama street on his way
home.
Both shootings were without the slight
est cause. Westmore entered the Mont
gomery Hotel shortly after 7 o'clock very
much under the influence of liquor and in
a very obstreperous mood. He went to
Halversen, at the night clerk's desk, and
. demanded the key to a side entrance of
the hotel, saying that he intended to
bring in a friend. Halversen. perceiving
that w estmore was intoxicated, refused
to give him the key, and Y^estmore start-
